

# --- A Municipal History of Boulder 1871-1946

by  
Lynn I. Perrigo

---

Manuscript copied with the author's permission by the BOULDER PUBLIC LIBRARY FOUNDATION.  
Cover drawing from *Remembered Yesterdays* by Eben G. Fine.



Lynn I. Perrigo is an Emeritus Professor of History of New Mexico Highlands University.

A native of Indiana, he taught in public schools from 1921 to 1926 and then served for six years as executive of the Boy Scout Council in Muncie, Indiana. After receiving his B. A. degree from Ball State University in 1933, he completed work for his Ph. D. at the University of Colorado in 1936, for which his dissertation was a history of Central City.

Following his employment for nine years as a professor in the University of Kansas City, he was granted a leave in 1945 to 1946 to research and write this history of Boulder, with emphasis upon the municipality, under the joint sponsorship of the Boulder County Historical Society and the City of Boulder. Next, after a year with the University of Colorado Extension Division, he was appointed head of the Department of History and Social Sciences at New Mexico Highlands University and continued there until his retirement in 1971. He now resides in Roswell.

He is the author of seven books, 40 articles in professional journals, and several series of articles in newspapers, including the Boulder Daily Camera. For one of his recent books, from the press of the Pruett Publishing Company in Boulder, he received the Perez de Villagra award from the New Mexico Historical Society.

This transcription was prepared from a copy of the original manuscript and is a project of the Boulder Public Library Foundation, Inc. Through an agreement with the author, permission for reprinting any portion of this manuscript should be obtained from the Foundation, 1000 Canyon Boulevard, Boulder, Colorado, 80302.

The transcriber gives thanks to the following for their assistance in preparing this copy: To Jack Quinn for his hours of help with the computer and the printer; to Lois Anderton for providing a basic index; to Clark Richert for his cover design; and to Marcelee Gralapp for her encouragement and support.

The responsibility for any errors in the transcription or typing is mine.

Virginia Braddock  
October, 1986

## A MUNICIPAL HISTORY OF BOULDER, COLORADO 1871-1946

By

Lynn I. Perrigo  
Assistant Director  
Bureau of Class Instruction  
Extension Division  
The University of Colorado

Sponsored by  
The Boulder County Historical Society  
and  
The City of Boulder

1946  
Seventy-fifth Anniversary



## PREFACE

The preparation of this history was made possible by the interest of the many citizens of Boulder who have lent support to the local historical society, and through the interest of the city council and city manager, who responded to the invitation for the City to join with the Boulder County Historical Society as co-sponsor.

This year, 1946, marks the seventy-fifth anniversary of local municipal organization, and the preparation of this volume during the past eight months was authorized as a part of the observance of that anniversary. It is hoped that this part of the observance will have lasting value and interest. The work has been facilitated by the generous cooperation of municipal department heads and former officials who were interviewed and who in some instances made accessible invaluable materials. Encouragement and advice were contributed by the members of a special committee appointed by the historical society, namely:

Dr. Colin B. Goodykoontz  
Eben G. Fine  
Dr. Percy S. Fritz  
H. C. McClintock  
Dr. Clay P. Malick  
A. A. Paddock

In addition the preliminary draft was read and criticized by:

Dr. Percy S. Fritz, Chapter One,  
Emil A. Johnson, Chapter Four,  
H. C. McClintock, Chapters Four through Six,  
Frank L. Moorhead, Chapters Two through Six,  
A. A. Paddock, Chapters One through Six.

This brief acknowledgment is inadequate to express my gratitude for this interest and assistance, which has contributed much to the fruition of this project. However, responsibility for any errors or defects in the work must be mine.

Lynn I. Perrigo  
Boulder, Colorado  
May 25, 1946

## CHAPTER ONE

### IN BOULDER VALLEY

Hail, rocky heights! who's storm-cleft snowy range  
Stands proud, majestic, far above the plain;  
Time's own eternal hand has wrought the change  
That only Time's eternal hand can change again;  
How oft in childhood have I read of thee,  
And read and wondered with a child's delight,  
Nor dreamed that I, in age, thy face might see,  
The magic wonders of the Eternal's might.

And here the loveliest of thy hamlets stands,  
The spot of all thy mountain cities best  
Bedeck'd with blessings from kind nature's hand,  
Nestling in quiet 'neath thy mountain crest.  
And lovely Boulder, I with weary tread,  
Must bend me on my long and weary way,  
And wait the hour when with the silent dead

I'll view thy beauty in eternal day.

--H. Ferrard, M.D., in the Boulder County Pioneer,  
February 10, 1869.

Where the Rocky Mountains rise abruptly from the plains about twenty-five miles northwest of Denver a community of over 15,000 people has become established during the years since a band of pioneers first made camp there in the fall of 1858.

The site is unique, because at this point it is only about twenty miles from the foot hills to the Continental Divide.<sup>1</sup> In the lower range, North Boulder Creek, carrying off the rains and thaws of the eastern slope, has worn a deep and scenic canon in the granite. As the creek comes out of

1. Amos Bixby, "Boulder County," in History of Clear Creek and Boulder Valleys, Colorado (Chicago: Baskin and Co., 1880), page 381.

the canon onto the plain it passes through a gateway formed by the monumental Red Rocks on the north and the towering Flagstaff Mountain on the south. At this gateway, in the broad valley of Boulder Creek, is the city of Boulder, so-named by the pioneers because the ground was strewn with granite boulders of all sizes. Although this site was in territory claimed by Spain from 1494 to 1800 and by France from 1800, or even earlier, to 1803, and then by the United States after 1803, as a result of the Louisiana Purchase, the Arapahoe and Southern Ute Indians had enjoyed undisputed possession until Thomas Aikins and his party of prospectors started building their cabins at the foot of the Red Rocks in mid-October, 1858. These pioneers were looking for gold, but they and their successors recognized soon that here was a spot with a variety of resources and advantages--mineral, agricultural, industrial, scenic, and salubrious.<sup>2</sup>

The first band of permanent settlers encamped at the foot of the Red Rocks on October 17, 1858, and erected eleven log cabins there yet that fall.<sup>3</sup> Then they turned

2. Boulder County probably has a greater variety of resources than any other county in Colorado. --James F. Willard and Colin B. Goodykoontz, Experiments in Colorado Colonization (University of Colorado Historical Series, II, 1926), pages 252-3; although 51st in area the county ranks among the first ten in metal mining, coal mining, manufacturing, raising dairy cattle and production of oats, barley, wheat, sugar beets, and fruit--Percy S. Fritz, "Boulder County," in Who's Who in Colorado (Boulder: Colorado Press Assoc; 1938), page 78.
3. Ibid., page 75.

to prospecting and on January 16 made a rich discovery at Gold Run in the mountains. Thus confident about the future, they organized the Boulder City Town Company on February 10, elected A. A. Brookfield president of the company, laid claim to a townsite extending for two miles along Boulder Creek, and divided it into 4044 lots.<sup>4</sup> Although some shareholders wished to attract settlers by offering the lots at reasonable prices, the speculative sentiment prevailed and the lots were held at \$1,000 each. They continued to be held, too, for there were few takers at that price; consequently the

Brookfield, formerly mayor of Nebraska City, wrote on March 5, 1859: "we thought as the weather would not permit us to mine we would lay out and commence to build what may be an important town," Hafen, L. R. ed., Colorado Gold Rush; Contemporary Letters and Reports (Glendale, California: Clark, 1941), page 248. Following are the names of the 60 men who organized the town company and held title to the first 60 lots in Boulder: James Able, Thomas Aikins, Isaac S. Bull, John S. Bull, John L. Buell, Wm J. Buckwalter, J. P. Brown, Albert E. Baugh, George Briggs, Daniel Blocher, Albert Baber, Silas D. Burns, A. A. Brookfield, D. S. Corbin, George Becknel, H. N. Childs, Charles E. Cook, Albert Corbin, Nicholas Connelly, Wm Cheatley, M. D. Cowser, John Cassity, C. M. Clouser, Louis C. Davenport, George Dixon, D. J. Ely, O. P. Goodwin, S. D. Hotchkiss, David Horsefall, R. N. Harris, George Hackleman, John R. Hall, T. R. Hamilton, N. K. Hughey, N. N. Jones, A. F. Kennedy, Thomas Lorton, John McKay, Alfred Miller, W. B. Moore, James P. M<sup>c</sup>Chesney, Dennis McManus, John Morlin, A. B. Perington, John Rotheric, R. L. Simpton, B. A. Sinn, Wm Scowfield, Theodore Squires, J. T. Tray, W. W. Towner, J. E. Williams, James Wagstaff, D. Wagstaff, E. S. Warren, G. R. Williamson, D. M. Woolley, T. J. Younker, G. W. Thorp, I. D. Henderson. From the Journal of the Boulder City Town Co., 1859 to 1861, pages 33-4.

permanent development of Boulder was retarded from the start.<sup>5</sup> Probably several thousand prospectors were in and near "Boulder City" in the summer of 1859, but only a few more log cabins and stores were built in town,<sup>6</sup> and instead Gold Hill enjoyed a boom.<sup>7</sup> That fall many of the prospectors sensed the opportunity for farming and preempted ranches. The next summer many of them had better success than the miners, who were running into difficulty in extracting gold from the ores.<sup>8</sup>

The miners in the mountains had organized mining

5. Fritz, in Who's Who, page 75; Frank Hall, History of the State of Colorado (Chicago: Blakeley, 1889-95), III, pages 292-3; Ella May Sanborn, in Boulder Daily Camera, Jan. 15, 1942.
6. In February John L. Buell reported that 60 to 75 houses were being built, Hafen, Contemporary Letters, page 241; in June Horace Greeley saw only a "log hamlet of some thirty habitations," An Overland Journey (N.Y.: Saxton, Barker & Co., 1860), page 167, and in August the Rev. Jacob Adriance counted only 10 or 12 log houses, and "none completed," letter to the Rev. John A. Davis, April 14, 1904. Nevertheless a reporter wrote to the Rocky Mountain News, October 13, 1859, that there were "sixty well built houses"; and it was said later that although many people moved their houses to ranches there were 60 houses still left the next spring, J. W. Hamm, Boulder County Directory (Boulder Publishing Co., 1896), page 115. The Boulder House, razed in 1897, was built in 1859 for a grocery store, Camera, June 15, 1897.
7. Mabel Guise Montgomery, A Story of Gold Hill (Author, 1930), pages 7-8; history of Gold Hill, Boulder County News, July 14, 1876.
8. Hamm, Directory, 1896, page 111; Bixby, in Clear Creek and Boulder Valleys, pages 382-9; H. H. Bancroft, History of Nevada, Colorado, and Wyoming, 1540-1888 (San Francisco History Co., 1890), page 576; story of the Wellman brothers, Boulder County News, March 16, 1877.

districts in order to protect their land claims and to maintain order,<sup>9</sup> and now the farmers in the valley also organized claims clubs, with secretaries who recorded the land claims and their boundaries.<sup>10</sup> After 1860, then, the initial boom and enthusiasm had subsided and Boulder had settled down for the long pull--agriculture by irrigation, and scientific development of mining and milling, in the face of some discouraging obstacles.

Manifold difficulties almost overwhelmed the pioneers in the decade of the 1860's. There were no good, direct roads connecting the agricultural and the mining regions. To Denver and the East, too, transportation was by stagecoach and freight wagons. At first there was no government with any authority, outside of that spontaneously organized in the mining districts. In addition mining awaited the investment of capital and the building of mills, and farming was set back by the grasshopper plague in 1861. To make matters worse, all efforts to gain some headway were hindered for four years by the effects of the Civil War, and at the end of

9. The first mining district, Mountain District No. 1, was organized sometime prior to July 23, 1859, Montgomery, Gold Hill, page 11; Percy S. Fritz, Mining Districts of Boulder County, Colorado (Boulder: University of Colorado thesis, 1933), page 15, and "Constitutions and Laws of Early Mining Districts in Boulder County, Colorado" University of Colorado Studies, Vol. XXI, No. 2, page 109.
10. Sanborn, in Camera, March 21, 1940; Fritz, in Who's Who, page 75.

addition a system of county roads was planned and several of them were opened.<sup>18</sup> For local government, after the pioneers had voluntarily organized Jackson County in the Territory of Jefferson, the Congress responded by formally establishing the Territory of Colorado in 1861, and Boulder County was organized that November.<sup>19</sup> Then after the trials of the Civil War had passed, the Territory and the County both organized for defense against the Indian menace, which was soon abated.<sup>20</sup> In those years a few more stores and mills were built, schools, churches, and lodges were started, an agricultural society was organized, the first newspaper was founded, and the county built the first small court house.<sup>21</sup>

When Bayard Taylor visited Boulder County in July, 1866, he came on horseback from Denver by way of the "Salt Lake stage road." At Marshall he stopped to see the "massive smelting furnace" and the coal mines. Then, on his way to Valmont, "For miles farm followed farm in uninterrupted succession, the breadths of wheat, black-green in richness, or overrun with yellowing gleam, were dotted with houses and

18. Minutes of the Board of County Commissioners, April 10, 1862; Jan. 11, 1864; Jan. 3 and April 7, 1865; Jan. 13, 1866; and many other entries.

19. See the next chapter.

20. Bixby, loc. cit., pages 397-400; and reminiscences of George Mack, Camera, Oct. 17, 1945. Marinus G. Smith was captain of the Boulder home guards, biography, in Clear Creek and Boulder Valleys, pages 679-80.

21. See the latter part of this chapter, on institutional development.

that war by the Indian uprisings on the plains.<sup>11</sup>

Although historians have written that "for ten years the town hardly held its own, and little of historical interest occurred,"<sup>12</sup> there were none the less a few significant achievements. In the first place the development of agriculture and irrigation made good progress,<sup>13</sup> and although mining practically stopped at Gold Hill,<sup>14</sup> there was considerable activity in Gilpin County and some in other camps.<sup>15</sup> A road was built up Gregory Canon to Black Hawk in 1862, and after it washed out another was opened up Boulder Canon to Four Mile and thence up Magnolia Hill to South Boulder Creek and the Enterprise road, which went to Central City.<sup>16</sup> After it was finished in 1866 it connected the farms in the valley with the market in the mountains.<sup>17</sup> In

11. Frank Fossett, Colorado, Its Gold and Silver Mines (N.Y.: Crawford, 1879), pages 130-146; Percy S. Fritz, Colorado, the Centennial State (N.Y.: Prentice-Hall, 1941), chapters IX and X. Also, on the grasshopper plague, see the Camera, Feb. 26, 1926.
12. Bixby, in Clear Creek and Boulder Valleys, page 403; copied in Hamm, Directory, 1896, page 115, and in Boulder County Directory, 1892 (Trinidad, Colo: Benschel Directory Co., 1892), page 20.
13. Ibid., pages 386-392.
14. Note 7, above.
15. Note 11, above.
16. Martin R. Parsons, "Toll Roads in Boulder County", Camera, April 2-5, 1945; Rocky Mountain Directory and Colorado Gazetteer, 1871 (Denver: Wallihan), page 127; Bixby, loc. cit., pages 392-5.
17. In the year 1869 to 1870 the road collected \$2,100.10 in tolls, which at one dollar a wagon represented a fair traffic. Boulder County News, July 20, 1870.

clumps of trees, like some fenceless harvest-plain of Europe." At the new settlement of Valmont he found a dozen cabins, a store, a printing office, and a Presbyterian church, located on "the prettiest village site in Colorado."<sup>22</sup>

Although he neglected to say much about Boulder, a fair description of it is provided by a photograph taken that year. According to the newspaper comment in 1889 on that old photograph: "The first house in the foreground is the old Colorado House. In the enclosure to the west is a pigstye. Then comes a frame shanty now occupied by the Chinese laundry. The next building is the Macky-Dabney block. Beyond this are two small frame shanties, and then the western portion of the Boulder House. Beyond this are the mountains."<sup>23</sup>

A few years later, in 1869, even though the newspaper boasted that the total agricultural and mineral production of the county was valued at about \$671,000, it also published a delinquent tax list of 202 names, including many of the leading citizens.<sup>24</sup> Boulder needed a second impulse.

After 1869 several factors stimulated a renewal of activity. One was the founding in 1869 of the third local

22. Colorado, A Summer Trip (N.Y.: Putnam, 1867), pages 154-160.

23. Boulder County Herald, March 20, 1889.

24. Boulder County News, Nov. 23, 1869; April 13, 1870.

newspaper, an aggressive one, which boosted and applauded every development.<sup>25</sup> Another was the opening of rich silver mines at Caribou in 1869 and the discovery of tellurium at Gold Hill in 1872, which caused another and less transitory burst of mining activity.<sup>26</sup> Immediately the toll road was built up through the difficult narrows of Boulder Canon to Caribou, and this brought the mountain trade to Boulder.<sup>27</sup> In addition the proposal to locate the state university at Boulder, dormant since 1861, came to life and was on its way to fruition in 1877.<sup>28</sup> These developments were sufficient to call for the organization of a town government in 1871,<sup>29</sup> and at that time the county as a whole also benefitted by the founding of the town of Longmont as the center for a prosperous agricultural colony.<sup>30</sup> The next year a county immigration society was organized for active promotion of settlement.<sup>31</sup>

25. The Boulder County News. A summary of newspaper history appears later in this chapter.

26. Treated more fully later in this chapter. Also, Fossett, Colorado, 167.

27. Note 16, above. When the road was completed, the Colorado Stage Co. put on it a tri-weekly stage, fare \$3.50 to Caribou. Boulder County News, May 27, 1871. In those years the county was actively pushing road-building in all directions. Minutes of the County Commissioners, 1869 to 1871.

At that time, 1871 to 1872, two railways were building toward Boulder. The Denver and Boulder Valley, although started in 1870, stalled upon reaching Erie, and even after Boulder citizens put forth strenuous efforts to push it to completion, did not run a train to Boulder until in September, 1873.<sup>32</sup> In the meantime the Colorado Central, coming up from Golden, had reached Boulder in April of that same year, thus providing Boulder, and Longmont, too, with their first railway outlet.<sup>33</sup>

All these stimulating factors, especially the completion of the railroads, contributed to the growth of Boulder and of the county in the 1870's.<sup>34</sup> Numerous visitors from afar registered each day at the Colorado House and Boulder House;<sup>35</sup> town lots sold rapidly at "fabulous" prices;<sup>36</sup> no houses were available for rent;<sup>37</sup> new

33. Ibid., Part II, Chapter IV; Bixby, loc. cit., page 393; Minutes of the County Commissioners, Jan. 3 and June 5, 1872; editorials and news items in the News, July, 1872, to April 18, 1873.

houses and store buildings were erected feverishly;<sup>38</sup> and real estate promoters laid out seventeen additions to the town between 1870 and 1878.<sup>39</sup> A map of Boulder in 1880 shows that it was then bounded on the north by Bluff Street and on the south by Water Street east of Twelfth and by Marine Street west of Twelfth, while from west to east it extended from Eighth Street to Nineteenth, with one section reaching on east to Twenty-fourth.<sup>40</sup> The people living in that area numbered over 3,000, whereas in 1870 and in 1860 the population had been only a little over 300.<sup>41</sup> It was in

this period, too, that the town organization effected in 1871 blossomed into reorganization and then to incorporation as a city of the second class in 1882.<sup>42</sup> Moreover, the prevailing optimism was reflected in the incorporation of several more railroad companies. The editor of a local newspaper boasted: "When the Denver Western & Pacific, the Denver, Utah and Pacific, the Boulder and Denver Airline, the Boulder, Left Hand and Middle Park, and the Boulder, Central and Utah roads are completed, Boulder county may indeed be called a railroad county."<sup>43</sup>

At times during that fruitful decade of the 1870's, visitors who came to Boulder wrote accounts of their observations. One, J. H. Tice, visited the mines at Caribou and the iron works at Marshall and observed irrigation and agriculture, in 1871, but left no description of Boulder itself.<sup>44</sup> Another, Grace Greenwood, who came in 1872, merely remarked about the "picturesque little town, nestled like a darling up against the mountains."<sup>45</sup> Charles Harrington, after his visit in 1874, was more communicative than the others:

42. Treated in Chapter Two.

43. Herald, March 23, 1881.

44. Over the Plains and on the Mountains (St. Louis: Industrial Age, 1872), pages 90-139.

45. New Life in New Lands (N.Y.: Ford & Co., 1873, page 404.

"The city of Boulder is situated at the opening of Boulder Canon, and controls, to a great degree, the business of the mountain region, which is by no means meagre. It is not a handsome city, but still has many characteristics of a lively, active, energetic commercial center. It is not large, the population not exceeding two thousand, yet it is composed in the main of that class of early settlers, who, by their persistency, gave the place a habitation, as well as a name, and have seen it develop into a prosperous community."<sup>46</sup> To this another traveller added in 1879 that "Business seemed active and the place manifested thrift and enterprise."<sup>47</sup>

Reports from the community expressed pride and optimism. One citizen wrote anonymously in 1875:

"Our beautiful town, heretofore known as a quiet, unassuming, agricultural town, and one of those places which have hitherto supplied the adjacent mining camps with partial supplies, has within the past year presumed to greater achievements, and hence has largely increased the facilities for her rapidly increasing business, until there is no demand coming from our many prosperous mining communities but that can be supplied. Another change, quite as noticeable and

46. Summering in Colorado (Denver: Richards & Co., 1874), pages 49-50.

47. S. Anna Gordon, Camping in Colorado (N.Y.: Author's Publishing Co., 1879), page 84.

requisite as the former, is the way public spirit and enterprise has taken hold of the people, and we may expect many good things will yet be done to make our beautiful and healthy town more attractive."<sup>48</sup>

Finally an appropriate comment upon this awakening came from a local editor:

"Ever and anon is heard the cry in Boulder that it has too many old fogies, that its business men lack enterprise, that the town needs some first class funerals, and remarks similarly entertaining, instructive and respectful.

"When the town had but a handful of people its enterprise and liberality secured the construction of the B.V. road, and the C.C. was soon obliged to follow. The railroad spirit, to borrow the idea of Mr. Bixby, then crowded out the wheelbarrow spirit and the town took a step forward. Following on the heels of this a handful of men, scarce a hundred we believe, raised eighteen thousand dollars to secure the State University near the limits of this town. Was this not liberality? Was this not enterprise?

"Instead of having old fogies here we have a more go ahead, energetic, enterprising and charitable disposed set of men than can be found in any town of its size, east or west."<sup>49</sup>

48. Letter from "O. C. Casional," Daily Denver Times, March 20, 1875.

49. Boulder County Herald, Dec. 7, 1881.

In the 1870's Boulder's many advantages had begun to have effect, and growth since that time has been controlled by developments in several fields of activity.

Mineral production, which had been down around \$50,000 a year in the 1860's climbed steadily upward after the discovery of rich silver and tellurium deposits<sup>50</sup> and reached a peak near \$500,000 for Boulder County in 1879 and 1880.<sup>51</sup> Then a slump set in and lasted until gold mining picked up in the 1890's and sent annual production up to a new high, over one million dollars, in 1892. After that the mining of precious metals declined steadily. Production was only a few hundred thousand a year after 1900, and in the 1920's it fell even lower. In 1930 the total was only \$18,000, but following the devaluation of the dollar in 1933, which put a premium on gold, production enjoyed a mild revival.

The above refers only to the precious metals, and fortunately sole dependence was not upon them. The coal mines at Marshall, developed in the 1860's, soon were supplying the region, and then the field of mining expanded over most of the southeastern part of the county. Production climbed steadily upward and of recent years has been above

50. Note 7, above. In 1871 the net profit of the Caribou mine was reported to have been \$174,966.72. News, Sept. 1, 1871.

51. The data in this paragraph are from: Fritz, Mining Districts, pages 177-8; Fritz, in Who's Who, page 76, historical article, Camera, Jan. 28, 1926; and Erika Olga Stoeckly, Boulder: A Study in Historical Geography (University of Colorado thesis, 1938), page 7.

a half million tons a year.<sup>52</sup> In addition the yield of mica after 1882, of radium ores since 1918, and of fluorspar following 1920, have contributed considerably to the prosperity of the county,<sup>53</sup> and tungsten, discovered in 1900, has given the county a rich source of wealth worked to the limit during the two world wars.<sup>54</sup>

Usually classed with mineral production is petroleum, and Boulder had an oil boom, too. Signs of oil were found in the 1890's,<sup>55</sup> but the first producing well pumped about seventy barrels a day in January, 1902.<sup>56</sup> Shortly there were 117 companies selling stock and buying up land in this field, and excitement ran high.<sup>57</sup> Several wells did produce a high grade of petroleum, and in 1903 the county shipped 44,000 barrels.<sup>59</sup> But then the production and promotion tapered off to almost nothing, and attempts to strike oil again, after

the first World War, met with poor success.<sup>59</sup>

It is evident, then that if Boulder had been dependent solely upon mineral production, the growth of the city certainly would have experienced great fluctuations. However, other more stable resources were being developed too.

After the favorable beginnings in agriculture, irrigation facilities were added<sup>60</sup> and then Boulder County forged ahead, especially in the production of fruits, cereal crops, dairy products, and livestock.<sup>61</sup> This led to the founding of an agricultural society which sponsored its first county fair in 1869.<sup>62</sup> Five years later it was reorganized as an industrial society, which continued to hold the annual fairs for many years.<sup>63</sup>

By 1900 the county had 967 farms, averaging 100 to 175 acres each, and the value of marketed production was near one

million dollars, which put this industry far ahead of the mining of precious metals.<sup>64</sup> At that time nearly nine-tenths of the improved acreage was under irrigation.<sup>65</sup> After 1900 another asset was added by the development of sugar beet culture, centered at Longmont.<sup>66</sup> Thus agriculture continued to expand until in 1940 nearly one-half the area of the county was in farms, averaging about 150 acres in size and valued altogether at about twelve million dollars.<sup>67</sup>

Along with these developments in mining and farming Boulder also attracted a few manufacturing industries. The first, of course, were related directly to local resources and needs, as sawmills, flour mills, stamp mills for ores, and an iron blast furnace at Marshall.<sup>68</sup> In 1871 the county had only twenty-one small industrial establishments employing

fifty-three persons,<sup>69</sup> and the failure to take full advantage of the opportunity was lamented by some aggressive citizens. A local editor wrote: "We have lain idle, half asleep as it were, so long that we are letting many chances slip while other towns in our territory are taking advantage of the sloth and indolence manifested by us and are reaping a rich reward."<sup>70</sup>

To the small shops at Boulder were added some larger ones, later, as flour mills, smelting plants, machine shops, brick manufactures, breweries, and for a while, an oil refinery.<sup>71</sup> However, in the 1890's a conflict arose between those citizens who wished to keep Boulder quiet and attractive and those who wanted industries with large plants and payrolls. Thus when a prospective industrialist was brought to the city, only to find meagre encouragement, one disappointed citizen protested: "Boulder must have factories and the only way to get them is to seize them when in sight. Sugar catches more flies than vinegar, and billious traitors to their own city and to their own families should be weeded out...."<sup>72</sup> As this contention continued on down through

the years the opposition generally prevailed,<sup>73</sup> and in 1940, with abundant power<sup>74</sup> and other resources, the city had only fourteen manufacturing plants producing goods valued under a half a million dollars a year, mostly cutlery and food products.<sup>75</sup>

One of the reasons for the opposition to manufactures was the headway which Boulder was making as an educational center. As early as 1861 the territorial legislature had provided that the state university should be located at Boulder, but little was done for ten years.<sup>76</sup> Then three townsmen donated the original site, they and others raised about \$17,000 to match a territorial appropriation, and "Old Main" was erected, ready for the first preparatory class in 1877.<sup>77</sup> By the fall of 1881 over 120 students were enrolled,<sup>78</sup> and three years later a medical department was

added and more buildings were started.<sup>79</sup> In 1892 the campus had eight buildings,<sup>80</sup> and soon Boulder could count as an asset more than \$50,000 a year spent by the state for the salaries of the thirty staff members.<sup>81</sup> An enrollment of 700 in 1904 was hailed enthusiastically, but that was only the beginning.<sup>82</sup> That year the summer session was initiated,<sup>83</sup> and further expansion of program and facilities continued.<sup>84</sup> The enrollment passed the 3,000 mark in 1928, and in the spring of 1946, under the impetus of post-war encouragement of education for veterans, registration reached a new high, above 5,000.<sup>85</sup>

In the meantime another educational institution also had been attracting students from afar. It was the Mount Saint Gertrude Academy, founded by four Sisters of Charity, B. V. N., in 1891, for "young children and girls who desire health as well as a primary education,"<sup>86</sup> and the facilities were expanded in 1919 by the addition of wings to the original building.<sup>87</sup> By 1940 it and the University had made Boulder a nationally known center for education.

One factor which attracted students also worked in other ways for the benefit of the city, and that was Boulder's advantage as a health and pleasure resort. The altitude, about a mile above sea level,<sup>88</sup> and the generally cool, dry summers and dry, sunny winters provided an ideal combination.<sup>89</sup> In addition, the scenery had its attractions. A visitor who travelled the winding road up Boulder Canon in 1874 was overawed: "Each turn brings to light new views,

The normal monthly temperature ranges from 32.7° in January to 71° in July, and the precipitation from .40 inches in January to 2.76 inches in April, while for the year the normal temperature is 50.6° and rainfall 18.12 inches. There are, also, on the average 189 clear days, 114 partly cloudy, and 63 cloudy each year. Stoeckly, Historical Geography, pages 34-5. Also, Camera, May 28, 1934. University of Colorado, Boulder County Studies, (Boulder: University, 1921), page 10; U.S. Bureau of the Census, Mortality Statistics, 1930 (1934), pages 5-25.

rivaling all others passed in picturesque groupings and effects. It would seem that here is the studio of nature where scenes that far excel all works of man's pencil are hung with lavish profusion upon the granite walls."<sup>90</sup> And another exclaimed, "a feast, an ecstasy, an intoxication of the sight."<sup>91</sup> Moreover, the attractiveness of the mountains drew this comment in 1867, concerning the front range:

"Its numerous streams escape through the feather edge of the Foot-hills by the abjectest canyons, worthless but as avenues of access; but above these the surface has been fretted by a thousand rivulets making down the Range until it is one vast park, chiefly covered with forest, but still presenting sunniest openings on the banks of clearest and most musical waters."<sup>92</sup> Later, when a railroad was built up the canons and high onto the front range, it became known to excursionists as the "Switzerland Trail."<sup>93</sup> Further interest in these mountains was manifested by the founding of a climbers' club in 1909, leading later to the annual trips to Arapahoe glacier, with 300 participating in August, 1945.<sup>94</sup>

The possibilities of making Boulder a resort, recognized quite early,<sup>95</sup> came to fruition in a prominent way first in 1895 with the founding of the Colorado Sanitarium,<sup>96</sup> which was followed by other, smaller health establishments in later years.<sup>97</sup> Another outgrowth, in part for a resort and in part for education, was the Texas-Colorado Chautauqua, established at Boulder in 1898.<sup>98</sup> For it the city provided the grounds and buildings,<sup>99</sup> except the privately owned cottages, and as the years passed it grew into a remarkable community institution. In 1927 over 45,000 people attended the various programs,<sup>100</sup> and in 1940 the officers could boast that of 750 chautauquas once in existence, this was one of the ten or so still in operation, because it had been kept "new and novel to meet changing needs."<sup>101</sup>

96. A branch of the Battle Creek Sanitarium, operated by the Seventh Day Adventists, Camera, May 15, and Sept. 13, 1895.

98. Crockett Ricketts, "History of the Chautauqua," Camera, Feb. 6, 8, 12, 1926.

99. See the chapter on "Boosting Boulder."

100. Camera, Sept. 8, 1927.

101. In 1939 there were 264 residents in the cottages, and for them and the visitors a program was offered from July 1 to Sept. 2, including plays, movies, social activities and excursions. Ibid., Jan. 2, 1940.

The presence of the Chautauqua helped Boulder attract excursions from other states,<sup>102</sup> as well as visitors from other parts of Colorado for holiday observances.<sup>103</sup> This trend grew into a considerable tourist trade following the advent of the automobile and the opening of the Rocky Mountain National Park in 1915.<sup>104</sup> In 1932 the government forest rangers estimated that during that summer there were 125,200 visitors in the Boulder region.<sup>105</sup>

With all of Boulder's "diverse natural conditions" at work, the town grew steadily but not spectacularly.<sup>106</sup> From 1880 to 1890 the population increased only about 300, but then came a spurt for a few years, and from 1890 to 1895 twenty-eight of the city's subdivisions were added.<sup>107</sup> In 1891, with oil rumors, a new brick factory, a horse railway, a paper mill, a proposed beet sugar factory, and new public buildings under way,<sup>108</sup> the editor of the Camera filled out a

In that decade the population of the city doubled, reaching 6,150, and the county passed 20,000.<sup>110</sup> Three years later, following the oil boom, the directory estimate gave Boulder a total of 10,126 people.<sup>111</sup> However, with industrialization soon slowed down, the total in 1910 was only 9,538, or about 11,000 including North and South

Boulder.<sup>112</sup> During the next twenty years little further gain was recorded,<sup>113</sup> and then during the hard times of the 1930's this city gathered population,<sup>114</sup> with an increase of about fifteen per cent. Finally a vigorous spurt during and after the recent war gave the city a generously estimated total of about 18,000 people by 1946.<sup>115</sup>

As more and more people became residents of Boulder County, and as social change multiplied their wants, it became possible for an increasing number of entrepreneurs to engage in business. Where there was but a small market in the first years,<sup>116</sup> retail sales by 1878 had grown to one million dollars annually.<sup>117</sup> A few years later the residents were served by ten grocery stores, forty-five other stores,

thirty craft and service shops, four hotels, four restaurants, two livery stables, and four lumber yards. In addition, the town had a full complement of professional people, as, doctors, lawyers, ministers, and teachers.<sup>118</sup> Down through the years the number of establishments increased and the specialties changed with the times;<sup>119</sup> yet in 1938 Boulder was described by one student as still being "a semi-rural market town."<sup>120</sup>

When men found business either bad or good, they wanted more of it. To this end Boulder merchants organized a Board of Trade which functioned off and on from 1882 to 1896.<sup>121</sup> They tried again, in 1905, by founding a Commercial Association which later became the Chamber of Commerce,<sup>122</sup> and it advertised Boulder far and wide while also laboring for civic improvements.<sup>123</sup> To obtain efficient management of municipal affairs, the business group in collaboration with others fostered the adoption in 1917 of a city charter which

122. Camera, Aug. 4, 17, 26, 1905.

123. Developed in the chapter on "general welfare".

introduced the Hare system of voting and the city manager form of administration.<sup>124</sup>

With the dawn of the automobile age, after 1910, one of the major improvements sought to make business better was the construction of surfaced highways and streets.<sup>125</sup> After the first World War, when the number of automobiles and trucks in the county had increased from 1,200 in 1915 to over 7,000 in 1924, such improvement became imperative.<sup>126</sup> By 1940 the county had nearly 600 miles of highways, of which 116 miles were surfaced. At that time the people of this county had 13,000 motor vehicles.<sup>127</sup>

In addition to business and means of communication, a city's places of residence contribute to the well-being of the townsmen. The pioneers, of course, started with crude log cabins,<sup>128</sup> but in the sixties most of them were replaced with frame and brick structures.<sup>129</sup> The first real boom in

building, however, came in the early seventies,<sup>130</sup> and the next one in the nineties.<sup>131</sup> Glimpses of Boulder in those years are provided by the descriptions written by visitors. According to one who came in 1878:

"The houses are so universally of brick that one might style it a 'brick city'. They are built mostly on the ground plan covering a large area, rather than rising up story after story.

"Gardens are universal, and the family which cannot show its large watermelons, immense squashes, the sweetest of corn, cucumbers, beans, etc., etc., can--depend upon its neighbors for such."<sup>132</sup> To this another added in 1892, that "Boulder is essentially a city of beautiful homes...."<sup>133</sup>

By the turn of the century the installation of many modern improvements, as running water, telephones, electric lights, and gas, were making many of the homes more convenient and attractive.<sup>134</sup> At that time building was moving up onto the terraces. New additions in the neighborhood of the University were filling up with residences

principally for the growing University community,<sup>135</sup> and some mansions erected on Mapleton Hill set apart that beautiful site as an elite district.<sup>136</sup> Then the modest, older houses east and west of the business district tended to become more and more the homes of the average man, the wage-earner, who in 1920, for example, could not afford much for finery from his weekly pay of \$28.60.<sup>137</sup>

Although few additions were made to the city after 1908, the building boom of the 1920's filled in most of the vacant lots in those already admitted.<sup>138</sup> This was followed by a slump in the 1930's and during the recent war,<sup>139</sup> so that with the influx of 1945 Boulder's 4,500 homes were far too few.<sup>140</sup> Then University students, veterans of the war, had to be housed in auto trailers, a throwback to pioneer days.<sup>141</sup>

The residents of Boulder have long taken pride not only in their homes but also in their schools, as testified by the fact that in the latter field the city has had two "firsts". In 1860 the townsmen, working under the direction of Abner R. Brown, the teacher of a private school, built the first school building in Colorado.<sup>142</sup> Later in 1876, Boulder had the first class to be graduated from a public high school in Colorado,<sup>143</sup> and by that time the original one-room frame structure had given way to a two-story brick building, dedicated in the spring of 1873.<sup>144</sup>

In 1877, however, when the University opened a state

preparatory school, it became Boulder's principal secondary school and continued to be after it was moved into a separate building on East Pearl Street in 1895, under a joint arrangement between School District No. 3 and the university.<sup>145</sup> Quarters more adequate were provided in 1906, and then the high school became less dependent upon the University.<sup>146</sup> It moved into the present modern, beautiful structure in 1937.<sup>147</sup> In the meantime, from 1882 to 1924, the city school district had added elementary and junior high school buildings to the number of eight,<sup>148</sup> and by 1940, the entire system, with about 2,600 pupils enrolled,<sup>149</sup> was organized on the 6-3-3 plan.<sup>150</sup> By that time, too, the city had three parochial schools with 274 pupils.<sup>151</sup>

In the ensuing decade, 1870 to 1880, seven more churches were organized in Boulder,<sup>155</sup> and presently the newspaper lamented that the town had "too many churches to support," causing some "simply to struggle for existence."<sup>156</sup> Nevertheless organization of more continued as the city grew, so that by 1945 it had twenty-five religious bodies.<sup>157</sup>

During those years, too, most of the churches erected attractive edifices.<sup>158</sup> All of this was stimulated by the rousing revivals conducted by Rev. Billy Sunday in 1909, and the Rev. E. J. Bulgin in 1914.<sup>159</sup>

Likewise, after a late beginning there was a rapid multiplication of lodges and other social organizations. The first was the Golden Sheaf Lodge no. 19, of Good Templars founded in September, 1868, and the second was Columbia Lodge No. 14, A. F. and A. M., which moved to Boulder from the Ward district in October of that year.<sup>160</sup> An Odd Fellows Lodge and a Grand Army Post followed in 1869,<sup>161</sup> a Y.M.C.A. by 1875,<sup>162</sup> and the Knights of Pythias, No. 12, in 1880.<sup>163</sup>

A second spurt of organizing came between 1900 and the first World War, so that the list in 1918 included the

Boulder Club, the Commercial Association, the Metal Mining Association, the G.A.R., the Chautauqua Association, the Elks, the Knights of Columbus, the Woman's Club, and some thirty fraternal societies.<sup>164</sup> To these have been added subsequently the American Legion, other veterans' organizations, and the popular luncheon clubs--Lions, Kiwanis, and Rotary.<sup>165</sup>

A considerable contribution to the building of Boulder and its society has been made by its newspapers. A number of them, including the first two, in 1867 to 1869, had short lives.<sup>166</sup> Those which have served the community long and well include the Boulder County News, which was founded in 1869 and continued to 1913;<sup>167</sup> the Boulder County Herald, which started in 1880 and merged with the News in 1913;<sup>168</sup> the combined News-Herald, which finally was absorbed by the Camera in 1932;<sup>169</sup> the Boulder County Tribune, published by the Camera as its weekly edition since 1891;<sup>170</sup> The Boulder County Miner, launched in 1901 and known as the Miner and

Farmer since 1920;<sup>171</sup> and notably, the Boulder Daily Camera, continuing since 1891 and edited by the late L. C. Paddock from 1891 to the time of his death in 1940.<sup>172</sup>

While aggressive leaders were drawing upon the advantages of the site to build a pleasant community at the foot of Flagstaff Mountain all did not go smoothly at all times. The difficulties did not vanish with the passing of the 1860's, and periodically the city has experienced several crises. Some were local in scope; others were counter parts of international crises. Epidemics of smallpox and other diseases were serious back in the 1880's before medical science provided controls.<sup>173</sup> In the 1880's, the development of the community was retarded by the feeble supply of murky water.<sup>174</sup> After that problem was solved for the time being, the city suddenly had too much water, for a bad flood in 1894 swept away all bridges and did considerable damage to residences and business houses.<sup>175</sup>

171. Files in the University Library.

172. Files in the University Library and Camera office. Biography of L. C. Paddock in Who's Who in Colorado, page 102, and in the Camera, July 31, 1940.

173. Especially the years 1888-9. Minutes of the City Council, Jan. 2, 1888; August 5, 1889.

174. Concerning the water problem in the 1880's see the Chapter on public services.

175. Camera, May 31, 1894. Before and after this disaster in Boulder the newspapers were reporting strikes at Cripple Creek and at Louisville, an assassination in France, a western Coxeys Army at Denver, Japan and China at war, two main railways in receivership, and several suicides at Boulder. It was an era of catastrophes. There was a second, less serious, flood in 1919. Camera, August 1, 1919.

Several years after recovery from the flood, Boulder went to war along with the remainder of the nation and most of the world. Although some businesses were curtailed during the first World War, the activity in tungsten mining more than made up the difference.<sup>176</sup> On the whole Boulder County did its part well in all the Red Cross and Liberty Loan drives,<sup>177</sup> and 773 men saw service, of whom fifty-one gave their lives.<sup>178</sup>

After that war the national boom and recklessness was reflected in this city,<sup>179</sup> and then it, with the rest of the nation, fell into the depths of depression in the 1930's. For the first time the problem of relief for the unemployed became so serious that national aid was welcomed.<sup>180</sup> On the whole, however, it must be conceded that the city benefitted in many ways during the depression, by the re-emphasis upon

education, by the acquisition of beautiful buildings through federal aid, and by the improvements made in the parks by the Civilian Conservation Corps.<sup>181</sup>

No doubt more serious than all crises was the second World War, which greatly restricted all business and building and drew heavily upon all resources, human and physical. With the remainder of the nation, Boulder had its Civilian Defense, salvage, rationing, financial drives, and conscription,<sup>182</sup> and when the war ended in Europe, 4,077 men and women from the county were in the Armed Forces, and seventy-seven from Boulder had given their lives.<sup>183</sup>

After victory over Japan the city also experienced another crisis in the local effects of the wave of strikes,<sup>184</sup> and in the appalling shortage of housing facilities<sup>185</sup> and of building materials, along with the accompanying fantastic inflation in real estate values.<sup>186</sup>

181. Plans for high school building, C.C.C. work in park, and P.W.A. grant for University buildings, Camera Dec. 2, 1933; Oct. 26, 1936. Boulder had not been affected seriously by the panic of 1883, 1893, and 1907. Herald, Aug. 15, 1883; April 23, 1884; Camera, June 29, July 20, and Oct. 10, 1893; Oct. 26, 1907.

However, the problems of 1945 to 1946 were being met, as had those which had arisen before, and Boulder was planning hopefully for an even greater future.<sup>187</sup>

O'er the wide-spreading plains rolled the emigrant trains  
 In the stirring old pioneer days,  
 When the ripples of heat danced on shimmering feet  
 'Neath the summer sun's quivering blaze.  
 And the brave light which broke from each valiant eye spoke  
 Of a courage that never would quail  
 As the hardy men pressed t'ward the goal in the West  
 In the dust of the Overland trail.

They are with us today, many grizzled and grey,  
 And their old eyes with pride are aglow  
 As they see how the state has grown famously great  
 From the wildness they found long ago.  
 When the pioneers meet how they love to repeat  
 The old story that never grows stale  
 Of the brave days of old when the gray schooners rolled  
 To the West on the Overland trail.

--By James Barton Adams, written for the  
 Semi-Centennial. Camera, Nov. 24, 1909.

### CHAPTER TWO

### TRENDS IN LOCAL GOVERNMENT

A sizeable community has to have dependable agencies, under public control, to render the residents numerous services and to protect their health and safety. To this end the adult citizens organize a municipal government to which they assign only those specific functions which they conclude from time to time that they want this agency to perform, and which are in harmony with the state constitution. In Boulder the form and functions of the city government are outlined in a Charter adopted in 1917 and subsequently amended in a few respects.<sup>1</sup>

The electors are all citizens over twenty-one years of age who have resided in the state one year, in the county ninety days, in the city thirty days, and in their precinct ten days.<sup>2</sup> Sometimes they vote on special proposals, as the matter of adopting a change in the rules, and property owners

1. The subsequent paragraphs on present organization are based upon Charter and Code of Boulder, Colorado compiled by Frank L. Moorhead (Boulder Publishing Co., 1925); H. C. McClintock, Annual Report, City of Boulder, Colorado, 1944 (no place or publisher, 1944); summary of the report, Boulder Daily Camera, April 5, 1945; Minutes of the City Council, for 1945; the Camera, 1945, passim; and Boulder County Studies, 1919-21, especially pages 128-154. Where other sources are used they are indicated by specific references in foot notes.
2. Also affirmed in Camera, October 6, 1945.

may cast ballots on the desirability of having the city incur indebtedness for the making of a specific improvement. Regularly every other fall the electors also choose three new members for six-year terms on the city council. In addition, if not satisfied with the action of the council, the electors may by petition hold a special election to initiate a new measure or to vote down something adopted by the council.

The councilmen receive no pay and represent no sections or parties. They are nominated upon the request of at least twenty-five of their fellow citizens, who must sign and turn in a petition making the nomination.<sup>3</sup> They are elected by the Hare system, which is designed to provide always that minority groups have some representation.<sup>4</sup> The voting is very simple, for the electors merely indicate their first, second, and third, or more, choices by the use of numbers, 1, 2, 3, 4, etc., but the counting of the votes is quite complex. The total number of ballots is divided by the number of places to be filled, and any candidate who gets more than that number of votes is elected. Any surplus votes of an elected candidate are not counted for him, but are

1. Explained in ibid., October 3, 1933.

2. In 1945 only eight cities in the United States were using the Hare system. Marguerite J. Fisher and Edith E. Starrett, Parties and Politics in the Local Community (Washington; National Council for the Social Studies, 1945), page 87.

credited to the second choices indicated on them. On the first count only one or perhaps none will have the required total. Then the one with the least number of votes is dropped and the second choices on his ballots are also counted for the candidates to which they go. Quite likely one or two of the higher ones then will have enough votes to be elected. Next another low contestant is dropped and his votes transferred. This procedure is repeated sometimes seven or eight times until finally three candidates each have a sufficient total to be elected.<sup>5</sup>

On the first day of January following the election the three new members of the council join with the six continuing members to form the new council, and they choose one of their members to be mayor and another acting mayor. In addition at that time they make appointments to fill vacancies on the city commissions and they name a city attorney, a police magistrate, and an auditor. At other times, as vacancies occur, they are responsible for the appointing of a city manager, or a councilman pro tempore.

Regularly on the first and third Tuesday evenings of each month, and at other times on call, the council assembles to serve as the board of directors for the municipality.

5. It's operation is explained in ibid., page 88; and in the Camera, November 3, 1941; November 7, 1945, and frequently preceding elections. Also see note 117, below.

They sit around the long table in the city manager's office, and, in the presence of a few reporters and occasionally a small group of visitors, they study detailed mimeographed reports, consider the city manager's recommendations, debate their differences of opinion, hear the pleas of representative citizens, get the advice of specialists, and finally adopt motions for or against certain proposals. Sometimes the sessions are tense and bristling, but generally they are conducted in a friendly and rather informal spirit. Occasionally they may be devoted in part to something actually entertaining, like the demonstration of parking meters which do everything but make change for a quarter.<sup>6</sup>

Each fall the council has a fixed routine. It must consider the manager's budget for the next year, hold a public hearing concerning it, adopt it, and then fix the tax levy required to raise the sum called for by the budget. At about that time, too, it is usually necessary to make some transfers of sums from one department to another so that the budget for the current year will balance. Further, if changes are being made in some of the salaries fixed by the council, then an ordinance must be adopted changing them. In addition, on election year the council names the canvassing board and the assistants to the city clerk, and then receives and orders recorded the returns as they are reported.

6. Observations at the meeting of April 16, 1946.

The city manager, employed by the council, is the city's full time superintendent and specialist on municipal affairs. His duties are manifold, but by and large he has six main responsibilities. First, he is advisor and administrator for the council, i.e., he makes recommendations to them and then carries out their wishes. Secondly, he is executive officer for the several departments, working through the department heads, who in turn are responsible to him. Third, he is the city's business manager or administrator of the budget. Fourth, he handles all relations with public utilities which have franchises. Fifth, he is a personnel manager, interviewing applicants, filling vacancies, recommending promotions, and studying personnel relationships among the seventy employees.<sup>7</sup> Finally, he is a public relations officer, who hears complaints, publishes reports, and delivers occasional addresses on municipal progress and needs.

Under this plan the mayor, relieved of administrative detail, serves principally as the presiding officer of the council, as the head of the police in times of emergency, as a counsellor for the manager, and as the official representative of the city at public events and ceremonies.

Working in close relationship with the mayor, manager, and council is the city attorney who is employed on a part-

7. Sixty employees in 1943, and fifteen more temporary, Municipal Year Book (Chicago; International City Managers Assoc., 1943), page 222.

time basis to give advice on legal matters, to draft most of the ordinances, and to represent the city in court whenever it is involved in legal proceedings.

Two official commissions relieve the council of many of the details of the administration of two departments. One is the Planning and Parks Commission of six members, with one term expiring each year. It recommends the purchase and development of park lands, and it collaborates with the planning consultant in the preparation of long range objectives for future growth and beautification of the city.<sup>8</sup> It also serves as a zoning board of adjustment. The city is divided into zones, each having its particular restrictions upon building and land use. Whenever a property owner wishes to plead that an exception be made to the zoning rules, he submits his appeal to the board of adjustment, which in a majority of cases has granted the request.

The other commission is the Library Commission of five members, of which one retires each year unless reappointed by the council. It administers the Library fund, supervizes the staff, and manages the property. The Public Library of 22,000 volumes is housed in a building erected in 1906 by virtue of a gift of \$15,000 from Andrew Carnegie.<sup>9</sup>

8. See the report of the consultant, S. R. De Boer, in Camera, February 5, 1946.

9. Discussed further in Chapter V.

Five other departments are administered by department heads working under the supervision of the city manager.<sup>10</sup> The Department of Public Service is headed by a city engineer, which office at present is combined with the position of city manager. This department plans the extension of the water and sewer systems, maintains the fifty-three miles of sewers, forty-three miles of water mains, seventy-five miles of streets, the bridges and alleys, and the 5,500 acres of public parks, makes surveys for streets and sidewalks and for the fixing of lot boundaries, and prepares any required maps of the city. The extensive equipment required for all this work is housed and maintained at the city yards, where the department offices also are located.<sup>11</sup>

The city manager is ex officio head of the Department of Public Safety. In this department the Police Division, headed by a chief, has a force of eight men--captain, sergeant, and six patrolmen--who are responsible for enforcing the city ordinances and for cooperating with other law enforcement agencies, as well as for maintaining complete records of arrests and complaints. This division operates a short-wave radio station, and the two patrol cars have two-way radios. The Police Magistrate, who is appointed by the

10. A functional outline is given in Boulder County Studies, 1919-21, page 152.

11. Polk, Directory, 1940, page 16; interview with H. C. McClintock, April 6, 1946.

council, and who also may be a justice of the peace, conducts the police court where offenders pay their fines or receive jail sentences and have the proper hearing of their cases if so required. The Police Magistrate is also responsible for the keeping of records and for the rendering of an account of all fines received.

The Fire Division, also headed by a chief, has twelve employees--two captains and ten firemen. The division not only fights fires and keeps records of all alarms and fire losses but also cooperates with the county in case of rural fires and engages in a ceaseless program of education for fire prevention. The division maintains its equipment, which includes three hose and chemical trucks as well as the county's pumper and hose truck, all housed in the two fire stations.

The Building Inspector, also in the Department of Public Safety, inspects all building remodelling and all new construction to see that it complies with the city building code. The purpose is to eliminate any fire or health hazards and to enforce the zoning regulations. This officer also cooperates with the Fire Division in the inspecting of business and industrial buildings for fire hazards.<sup>12</sup>

The Department of Public Health is headed by a director who makes sanitary inspections of dairies, inspects meats and

12. Report, in Camera, February 4, 1946.

other foods as well as the places where they are sold, tests milk and water, and investigates complaints regarding nuisances in the city. This department ordinarily employs a visiting nurse<sup>13</sup> who makes calls at needy homes under the supervision of the family physicians. The department also is responsible for the keeping of records of births, deaths, and burials.

The department of Public Welfare, once headed by a part-time director, and once active in many fields of endeavor,<sup>14</sup> has been reduced by the exigencies of war to a recreation division with a part-time director and a limited staff.<sup>15</sup> The program is a community project, planned by a recreation council comprised of representatives of the municipality, of the city schools, and of the Y.M.C.A. -- the three agencies which share the cost.<sup>16</sup> The facilities include the city parks and city playground equipment, and the municipally owned Hygienic swimming pool. The volunteer Civic Band and Orchestra is a special feature of the program, which also

13. None was employed for the remainder of 1944 after the resignation of Hester Rohwer. Camera, April 5, 1945.

14. See Chapter V.

15. Interview with H. C. McClintock, April 6, 1946.

16. Ellin Smith-Mracheck, Community Recreation in Five Small Cities of Colorado (University of Colorado thesis, 1940), pages 8-21; Camera, March 22, 1946.

includes swimming, hikes, softball, motion pictures, dances, and other recreational activities.<sup>17</sup>

The nerve center of all city departments is the Department of Finance and Records, headed by a full-time director who has two assistants. The director is also ex officio city clerk, treasurer, purchasing agent, and secretary of the council. The department maintains the minutes of council meetings and the record of ordinances, collects water rents, fees, and assessments, has custody of city funds, keeps the journals of receipts and expenditures,<sup>18</sup> receives requisitions for purchases, pays all salaries, wages, and bills, keeps watch over the departmental budget allotments, and sees that ordinances and notices are published in the newspapers. Periodically the petitions and records pertaining to regular and special elections are also handled by this department.

An auditor, engaged by the council on a part-time basis, makes a semi-annual examination of the records to see that all entries are posted properly. Then his analysis of the city's financial situation and his recommendations are reported to the council and published in the local newspapers.<sup>19</sup> In addition he makes an audit of the books of

17. Tabulated report in ibid., February 18, 1946.

18. Appropriation ledger, register of contracts, orders, and payrolls, register of cash received and disbursed, voucher register, general journal, general ledger, and others. Boulder County Studies, page 157.

19. For example, the report in Camera, February, 19, 1944, showing the finances in good condition.

of any responsible official who dies or retires before the expiration of the term of appointment.

The annual budget is based upon a revenue of a little above \$200,000, of which about \$125,000 comes from taxes. The greater part of this goes for operating expenses, and a small amount for interest payments, investments, and the retirement of general obligation bonds. The Water Department, which pays its own way, has separate accounts for its receipts and disbursements totalling over \$125,000 a year.<sup>20</sup>

The indebtedness of the city is about \$150,000, and of that amount \$100,000 is charged to the Water Department. This leaves a general indebtedness of only about one-half of one per cent. of the assessed valuation, whereas the approved limit is three per cent. All of the city's assets are valued at more than three million dollars, of which the largest items are the street paving and the water system and lands. The assets include an airport and a golf course, which are leased to operating corporations.<sup>21</sup>

20. For 1946 the operating expenses were estimated at \$129,904.24, and the tax levy was fixed at 12.35 mills, not including local improvement assessments. Ibid., October 16, 1945.

The Office of Finance and Records is located in a city hall of 1882 vintage, which also houses the police headquarters and the offices of the city manager, the building inspector, and the nurse. However, the electorate has approved the issuance of bonds for the building of a new city hall across from Central Park, and it will also accommodate the Library and a city museum.<sup>22</sup>

This condensed survey of present municipal organization provides a preliminary outline, a frame of reference, for a study of its development. Into the making of this present organization have gone many ideas borrowed elsewhere and also the experience, the leadership, the traditions of eighty-eight years of local government, including seventy-five years of municipal government in this community.

22. Prior to 1882 the town board met in the office of A. J. Macky, and for a short time in that of John A. Ellet. Minutes, Sept. 7, 1875; Feb. 19, 1878; June 3, 1878. In 1880 the office of attorney, clerk, water department, and town board were on the ground floor of Ellet's block. Ibid., May 4, 1880. Then a site was bought for a fire department building, ibid., July 11, Nov. 10, 1881; and the building was nearing completion that fall, with the first floor for fire apparatus and the second for the use of two fire companies and the city council, Herald, Nov. 23, 1881. In 1890 a lot was bought at 13th and Spruce for a new town hall which was never built, Council Minutes, Sept. 8, 1890. This lot was sold in 1892, and the old county offices and jail were acquired, ibid., August 1, 1892. In 1898 Alderman Hubbard reported the present building "not worth repairing," ibid., Jan. 3, 1898. In 1907 a proposal to build a city hall was voted down, ibid., April 15, 1907; and again in 1933, ibid., November 7, 1933. Finally authorized, 1217 to 171, ibid., Nov. 6, 1945.

The first agency of government in this locality, not counting that of the aborigines, was Mountain District No. 1, a spontaneous organization of miners established at Gold Hill sometime before July 23, 1859. At least ten other districts were organized by 1866 in what is now Boulder County, and all provided a form of government. The miners in open meetings elected officers, adopted ordinances, and held court. The miners' laws not only governed the claiming and working of mines but also made the rules for the keeping of order in the camps.<sup>23</sup>

The mining districts, however, did not provide a government for the ranchers on the plains.<sup>24</sup> Although they organized land claims clubs, these did not provide a form of government. Neither did the Boulder City Town Company, organized on February 10, 1859, for the platting of a town and speculation in town lots.<sup>25</sup> Consequently the settlers in and near Boulder City were interested in the proposal that some kind of territorial organization be effected. Since Congress did not respond immediately, delegates from the

23. Fritz, Mining Districts, pages 11 to 21, et passim.

24. The district nearest present Boulder was in Boulder Canon at Four-Mile Creek. Ibid., page 21.

25. Fritz, in Who's Who, page 75; Sanborn, in Camera, Jan. 15, 1942. The claims club formed by the settlers in the vicinity of Boulder was known as the "Great Western". Sanborn, ibid., March 21, 1940.

mountains and plains voluntarily founded the unofficial Territory of Jefferson in the fall of 1859,<sup>26</sup> and in October a year later at a public meeting at Boulder City the local men elected officers for Jackson County in that Territory of Jefferson.<sup>27</sup>

Within a year, however, Congress established the Territory of Colorado, which included Boulder County, and to T. J. Graham of Boulder City was then delegated the responsibility for serving as county chairman during the election of the county officers. This done, the county commissioners met for the first time on November 15, 1861, and Boulder Valley as well as the mountain districts finally had a duly authorized local government.<sup>28</sup> To the county officers the citizens of Boulder paid their taxes, made their pleas for improvements, submitted their deeds, transfers, and contracts for recording, and applied for licenses for certain types of business.<sup>29</sup> Upon the sheriff, justices of the

peace, and county court they depended for the maintenance of order in their community. The county commissioners granted permits in the nature of franchises to toll road companies and to irrigation ditch companies and later called elections for the approval of bond issues to aid the building of railways. In 1862 the commissioners also adopted a plan for a system of thirteen county roads, some of which went through Boulder City, and by 1865 several of the roads had been improved. In the meantime, in 1863, the commissioners had divided the county into school districts which were placed under the supervision of a county superintendent, and the next year they had organized the local militia for a campaign against Indian marauders.

With considerable business to transact, the county office building, a frame house rented of A. J. Macky since 1862,<sup>30</sup> was no longer adequate; consequently in 1865 the commissioners had a small county office building erected on Pearl Street.<sup>31</sup> Two years later that structure was enlarged to provide an adequate court room and jail,<sup>32</sup> and this building was used by the county until 1883 and later by the city police department until 1924.<sup>33</sup> The action of the

county commissioners in making Boulder City the permanent county seat was protested by the citizens of the new, rival town of Valmont, but to no avail.<sup>34</sup>

In the late 1860's the county was torn with sectional animosities and political strife. The editor of the newspaper at Boulder City charged that this town had cheated Valmont out of the court house, that a Democratic "ring" was running everything in favor of a few Boulderites and that the county was hopelessly in debt.<sup>35</sup> He claimed, too, that county officers had swindled the people out of proper financial returns from the sale of town lots, which had been turned over to the county by the Town Company in 1864 with the stipulation that revenue from their sale should be used to build a court house.<sup>36</sup> In 1869 the editor expressed his elation over a victory of the Independent candidates by exclaiming "The 'Ring' is bursted, sure; glory hallelujah!"<sup>37</sup> But then he was "bursted", too, for that turned out to be the last issue of his paper. Boulder men whom he had been

34. Minutes of the County Commissioners, July 6, 1865; The Denver Daily, April 15, 1867.

35. Boulder County Pioneer, April 28, May 26, June 9 and 30, July 7, and August 17, 1869.

attacking, and to whom he owed some money, had the sheriff take possession of this printing plant.<sup>38</sup>

In the midst of this turmoil a movement arose for the organization of a town government for Boulder City. As one man put it, people were "eager to cast this town out of their county."<sup>39</sup> However there were more fundamental reasons, mostly derived from the fact that Boulder City finally was coming to life. Railroads and a university were on the way, and town additions were being platted.<sup>40</sup> In view of the prospective growth the townsmen realized the need for a better supply of water and for protection against fire, two services not rendered by the county.<sup>41</sup> A few prominent citizens also had a stake in the future of the town, for they owned considerable real estate in and near Boulder City and naturally wanted the place to grow, preferably under their own management. Several of these men had long worked together in county politics;<sup>42</sup> they knew each other and

T. Pomeroy with the editor, J. N. Wharton, is recorded in Miscellaneous Record, County Clerk's Office, Volume I, pages 256-9.

adopted at some busy sessions during the next ten days, Boulder's first town government was fairly launched.<sup>48</sup>

A minor change in the form of organization followed seven years later, merely because Colorado had become a state in 1876 and the legislature had provided on April 4, 1877, for the incorporation of towns under state statutes instead of under county authority as before.<sup>49</sup> Consequently, in response to a petition signed by ninety voters, the town trustees on December 5, 1877, adopted an ordinance providing for the change.<sup>50</sup> When this was submitted at a special election on January 9, 1878, 214 of the 242 voters said "yes",<sup>51</sup> and on February 13 a new set of officers was elected to serve briefly, or until after the regular election in April.<sup>52</sup>

The only noteworthy change was that now instead of electing five trustees, a clerk, a street supervisor, and a constable, the electors chose a mayor, a recorder, and five trustees. Other offices were filled by appointment, as before, and as a result of the rapid growth of Boulder and the related expansion of its government in the 1870's, the

number of appointive offices was increasing rapidly.<sup>53</sup> Now for the first time the town had a "mayor", and Jacob Ellison, elected for a two months' term in 1878, was the first to be honored with that title.<sup>54</sup> Another change which took place at this time was the extension of the town limits to take in formally all the additions which had been platted since 1871,<sup>55</sup> but of course that could have been done regardless of the reincorporation.

In 1882, after the census for 1880 had shown the population of Boulder to be above 3,000, that made possible the next step, incorporation as a city of the second class.<sup>56</sup> This was effected merely by the adoption of an ordinance by the town trustees on April 3, 1882.<sup>57</sup> The principal

changes at this time were that the town was divided into four wards, that election procedure and salaries of officers were to be governed by state law for cities of this class, that the elective officers were henceforth mayor, treasurer and four aldermen, and that the aldermen were to serve for two years.<sup>58</sup> A new town hall, the present one, was completed in time for mayor and council of the new "city" to take possession in April,<sup>59</sup> and then they undertook an extensive revision of ordinances to bring them down to date and in harmony with the new form of government.<sup>60</sup>

The mayor-alderman plan adopted in 1882 continued in effect with only minor revisions until 1918. In 1892 the four wards were subdivided into eight voting precincts and the mayor thenceforth was elected for two years instead of one.<sup>61</sup> After 1903 eight aldermen were elected every second year and all served two-year terms until 1915 when four were

elected for two years and four for four years.<sup>62</sup> In the meantime, from 1903 to 1907, the city had experimented briefly with the electing of not only the council, mayor and treasurer but also the clerk, attorney, engineer, marshal, police magistrate, and street supervisor.<sup>63</sup> In addition, from time to time new appointive offices had been created<sup>64</sup> and ordinances and salaries had been raised to keep somewhat abreast of changing conditions.<sup>65</sup>

In the years from 1871 to 1917 city politics had two distinct characteristics. One was the tendency to form parties on the basis of local issues, and the other was the practice of using "mass meetings" for the making of nominations and the drafting of platforms.

After the county Democratic "ring" lost to Independent Republicans in 1869,<sup>66</sup> the county for some time consistently voted Republican.<sup>67</sup> In the Boulder City, however, at least a different title was used. The "Young Americans" nominated at

a "citizen's mass meeting," won over the other four tickets in the quiet first election in April, 1872.<sup>68</sup> The next spring the newspaper reported that there was "perfect indifference among the people as to who is elected for town trustees,"<sup>69</sup> and until the late 1870's there was little comment in the papers about town elections. Then the Republican News advocated that the "best rule to adopt in the selection of municipal or county officials is to choose such men as one would choose as trusted agents to manage any private business." As to politics, "We prefer not so much as to name politics in a town election. At any rate we prefer that the Republican party be not the first to force a political issue where all should unite, without regard to party, and devise for the public good."<sup>70</sup>

Since this was a mining county, it was natural that there should be a lively interest in the national parties which were formed around monetary issues. In 1877 a greenback club appeared in Boulder,<sup>71</sup> and the next spring the contest was between their "National Ticket" and a "Citizen's Ticket" and a "People's Ticket." Although the Greenbackers enlisted some prominent men, including the incumbent mayor,
