Jacob Ellison, only one of their candidates was elected.<sup>72</sup>

The next year again there were several local tickets, and the election "passed off quietly, there being no party issue nor special axes to grind."<sup>73</sup> With the founding of the Herald in 1880 the Democrats had a vocal agent,<sup>74</sup> and that year at the "citizen's" nominating convention the Republicans, finding "too many Democrats present," withdrew to make their own nominations.<sup>75</sup> At another meeting of "citizen's" they nominated a third, non-partisan ticket, which won all offices except that of mayor.<sup>76</sup> The Republicans kept trying to break up the non-partisan tradition and finally succeeded in filling all but one office in 1883.<sup>77</sup> Then when the victors squeezed out the one Democrat and named the defeated Republican, it created such a

row that the mayor resigned in disgust.<sup>78</sup>

For several years afterward a mixed combination continued to win city offices against the competing tickets, Democratic and Prohibition.<sup>79</sup> After 1887 the Republicans took over,<sup>80</sup> and in 1893 and 1894, when silver coinage was a vote-getting issue and the Populists were in county offices,<sup>81</sup> the Republicans had difficulty maintaining their control of the municipality, but still they managed to fill most of the offices.<sup>82</sup>

Allison resigned, the editor of the Herald, August 15, 1883, sympathized with him; "we cannot find it in our heart to blame him for his course in laying down an office the possession of which brought with it no satisfaction, but only harrassing, worry, criticism, fault-finding, and consequent vexation." Also see ibid., August 8, 1883, and Council Minutes, August 8, 1883. And Bounds was fined \$100 for assaulting the editor of the Herald. Herald, Oct. 10, 1883.

82. A Populist-Democratic combination with the popular George F. Fonda heading the ticket made a strong bid in 1893, but elected only one alderman. Ibid., March 14-18 and April 5, 1893. In 1894 they elected only George W. Teal. Ibid., April 3, 1894.

In 1897, after a new threat arose in the "Citizen's Reform League" which proposed to eliminate the saloons and commercialized vice,<sup>83</sup> the Republicans resorted to the old procedure of forming a "non-Partisan" ticket which was headed by the incumbent Republican mayor.<sup>84</sup> They won that year,<sup>85</sup> and subsequently they also beat Labor,<sup>86</sup> Socialist,<sup>87</sup> and Prohibition tickets,<sup>88</sup> until finally the various reform elements formed the "Better Boulder" party which defeated the "non-partisan" Republicans in 1907, thanks to the energetic support of women workers.<sup>89</sup>

83. The Citizen's Reform League ran Albert A. Reed against Crockett Ricketts, Republican, in 1895. Ibid., March 29, 1895.

84. Ibid., March 16, 19, 27, 1897.

85. The reformers elected only one alderman, Isaac T. Earl. Ibid., April 7, 9, 1897.

86. The Labor party elected one alderman in 1900. Ibid., April 5, 1900.

87. The Socialists ran a ticket the first time in 1903. Ibid., March 24, April 8, 1903. Afterwards a city policeman arrested one of the Socialist speakers for obstructing the sidewalk, and then when the Socialists demanded that some of their party be named election judges, the council refused on the ground that in all appointments parties were never "mentioned or thought of." Ibid., May 18, 1903, March 2 and 11, 1905; Council Minutes, Feb. 15, 1905. The Socialists were still active in 1909, with a platform which favored the initiative, referendum and recall, prohibition, city improvements to be done by the city (not contractors), a municipal light plant, civil service, a recreation program, and economy. Camera, Feb. 15, 1909. When the reformers were in office they allowed the Socialists to use the Council Chamber for meetings! Council Minutes, Dec. 10, 1910.

88. In 1898 the prohibitionists elected a clerk, attorney, police magistrate, and street commissioner. Camera, April 6, 1898.

89. A "Civic League" was organized in 1903 to "inject its membership into municipal campaigns in the interest of morality," Camera, Feb. 17, 1903. Later this became a prohibition party, and the victory of 1907 is discussed in Chapter V.

The "Better Boulder" party stood for morality, prohibition, observance of the Sabbath, political integrity, and municipal ownership of utilities, especially of the light plant.<sup>90</sup> As a reaction to this trend a movement arose for a "business" management of municipal affairs.<sup>91</sup> At that time the city manager plan, barely in the experimental stage, was not especially advocated in Boulder; yet obviously something of the sort was wanted.<sup>92</sup> Soon citizens petitioned the council for the calling of a charter convention to take advantage of a provision in the state constitution which permitted cities to adopt the form of government desired locally.<sup>93</sup> By that time news had spread about Galveston's commission form of government, and two cities in Colorado already had adopted it.<sup>94</sup> But this did not seem to be quite what the business group sought and their spokesman wrote: "Students of government have repeatedly advised the adoption

90. Treated more fully in Chapters III and V. Isaac T. Earl and E. D. Webb were the reform mayors.

91. In March, 1909, a meeting of those who had "had enough of Better Boulder" urged that a business man's ticket be elected to restore the city "to a business and progressive basis." Camera, March 3, 1909.

92. A "strong mayor" plan had been advocated since 1898 by the National Municipal League. In 1906 Staunton, Virginia, employed a "manager or superintendent," and the city manager plan was fully developed at Sumter, S. C., in 1914 and adopted by Dayton in 1914. H. A. Stone, Don K. Price, and Kathryn H. Stone, City Manager Government in the United States (Chicago: Public Administration Service, 1940), Chapter I.

of the commission form, but the average Boulder citizen is content, as long as the city is governed wisely, to be ruled by the old, but still efficient method."<sup>95</sup>

In other words one group did not like the existing system if the opposition was in control, but liked it if their own party was in power, and at that time the business element, or "Citizen's Party" had its candidate in office.<sup>96</sup> Consequently after a petition of some "reformers" for a vote on a charter convention was finally accepted by the council, on August 8, 1910,<sup>97</sup> the leading newspaper and the "Tax Payers' League" opposed it on the ground that the special elections and convention would cost \$14,000.<sup>98</sup> As a result the proposal was defeated by a vote of 1804 to 1129.<sup>99</sup>

The next year the reform party came back into power, and once more the business element began clamoring for a

This time, after Dean Milo Ketchum of the University had pointed out that under charter rule the city could employ a business manager,<sup>100</sup> the Commercial Association came to the support of the proposal.<sup>101</sup> However there was still strong sentiment in favor of a mere modification of the existing system by paying the mayor a better salary and expecting more of him.<sup>102</sup> Especially was this true after the Citizen's Party found and put into office a wealthy, retired business man, W. L. Armstrong, who gave almost full time to the city.<sup>103</sup> L. C. Paddock of the Camera supported the Armstrong administration,<sup>104</sup> and after a committee named by the Commercial Association had reported in favor of a council, mayor, and city manager plan, Paddock's motion to delay on account of the expense of elections was adopted by that organization and then by the city council.<sup>105</sup>

The movement did not lapse, however, and by September Paddock was ready to support it.<sup>106</sup> The following spring the council accepted another petition for another vote on this issue, and then the Camera, the Commercial Association, some ex-mayors, and several university professors supported the proposal, but in vain.<sup>107</sup> Its defeat, 832 to 676 was said to have been due to the bad weather and light vote, and also "to the satisfaction of the voters with the present Armstrong administration."<sup>108</sup> This time it was the city manager plan which was defeated, since this plan was then spreading over the country, and it was rather generally understood that the charter should provide for it.<sup>109</sup>

Council Minutes, Dec. 11, 1917. Boulder was the second city in the U. S. to adopt the Hare system, and since the first, Ashtabula, Ohio, later dropped it, Boulder now has used it longer than any other city in this country. Note 5, above, and News-Herald, March 30, 1923.

When the new council assembled on January 1, 1918, and elected F. C. Moys mayor and F. L. Moorhead acting city manager,<sup>118</sup> this event was hailed as the "beginning of a new era" in municipal affairs. With a woman on the council for the first time, the "big question" was "to smoke or not to smoke?" The next problem, according to one perennial Democrat, was to find a city manager with "the engineering ingenuity of General Goethals, the business acumen of William McAdoo, and the diplomatec wisdom of Woodrow Wilson."<sup>119</sup> From more than one hundred applicants, E. O. Heinrich, chief of police at Alameda, California, was appointed on January 21,<sup>120</sup> and when he took charge in February he said "I shall do nothing precipitate."<sup>121</sup>

After a difficult, busy year in office, Heinrich resigned, because, as he said, "One's country may inspire one to die for it, but a municipality is scarcely worth the supreme sacrifice."<sup>122</sup>.

118. Minutes, Jan. 1, 1918.

119. L. C. Paddock, in the Camera, Jan. 2, 1918.

120. Minutes, Jan. 1 and 21, 1918.

121. Camera, Feb. 11, 1918.

122. Ibid., April 2, 1919. Heinrich returned to his principal interest, criminology, and became a specialist of international repute. Also, a charge that he had been ousted. News-Herald, Feb. 19, 1923.

All was fairly quiet under his successor, W. D. Salter,<sup>123</sup> who held office for about a year, and for a while, too, under Scott Mitchell, a railway contractor who had been appointed manager in September, 1920, after eight years of residence in Boulder.<sup>124</sup> At that time Boulder's system of government was beginning to attract attention in other cities. A visitor from Tulsa, for example, after investigating several forms of municipal administration, praised that of Boulder for its "absolute lack of politics," and added that he had been more favorably impressed with the city manager form here than in any other city he had visited.<sup>125</sup> Soon afterward, however, a political storm which broke upon this community washed away its superficial tranquility, laying bare a deep cleavage.

There were three principal issues at stake in the fights over the Charter in 1923 and 1925. The first arose from opposition to the costly improvements, especially the extensive paving of streets, which were being launched in those years. In July, 1922, a group of women charged that

123. Salter had previously been city engineer, and after he resigned as manager he moved to a ranch in California Camera, Oct. 22, 1919; Council Minutes, July 27, 1920; News-Herald, April 22, 1925.

124. Camera, Sept. 1, 1920.

125. News-Herald, August 4, 1921. Another visitor from Palestine, Texas, quoted the mayor of Boulder as having said that the improvements under the manager plan were "simply marvelous," and that Boulder was receiving inquiries from all over the country as to its form of government. Ibid., Sept. 22, 1922.

the city and the Strange-Maguire Paving Company were conspiring to pave the entire city,<sup>126</sup> and then some property owners obtained an injunction to stop work on the Mapleton Hill project.<sup>127</sup> The next month the second and third issues appeared when a petition was circulated for the recall of the council members and for amendments to curtail the responsibilities of the city manager and to abolish the Hare system.<sup>128</sup> There was a fourth minor issue which was incidental to the others, and that was the extensive use of the "emergency clause" in the adoption of ordinances, which were thus put through quickly, without awaiting an opportunity for objections to crystallize.<sup>129</sup> It was this emergency procedure which led the local courts to grant the injunction to stop the paving of Mapleton Hill under ordinance No. 975, but then the council simply adopted a new ordinance, No. 976, identical except for the emergency clause.<sup>130</sup>

The first petition for a vote on amendments to the Charter was rejected by the city clerk, who, after marking

The assault on the city hall was led, odd as it seems, by F. J. Klingler, who once had campaigned vigorously for the Charter.<sup>134</sup> Now, however, he contended that "like all efficiency features of autocracy, we have since learned that these cannot be harmonized with the ideals of self-government and our American institutions."<sup>135</sup> A later bulletin added that the "Citizen's League,"<sup>136</sup> by advocating the amendments, was not opposed to the Charter but was "for the amendment of its weaknesses," was not wanting the manager system over-

The Citizen's League charged the city officials with conspiracy, with undue use of the emergency clause, with the contracting for inferior paving, and with the taking of a "rake-off" from the paving company.<sup>138</sup> The officials answered by publishing all of the bids and contracts, by circulating a statement issued by the paving company's representative, by pointing out all the gains under the manager plan, by referring to comparisons with other cities, and by printing a statement of the auditor that he had never found a "suspicion of graft."<sup>139</sup> A number of those who had

city attorney, Frank L. Moorhead, then became acting manager.<sup>146</sup> Still on the table, however, was the petition calling for a vote on Charter amendments at the next general election, that November, 1924.<sup>147</sup> Its sponsor, Victor I. Noxon, editor of the Miner and Farmer, in order to avoid a second election on the same issue within a year suggested that some of the signers withdraw their names so as to make that petition invalid, and this was done.<sup>148</sup>

Some others still would not give up. They circulated a new petition for reconsideration of the same set of Charter amendments,<sup>149</sup> and since this petition was properly prepared and had nearly 500 signatures, the city council accepted it and designated November 3, 1925, for the election.<sup>150</sup> This time the campaign to stop the paving of streets, to abolish the Hare system, and to make the manager "subordinate" to the council was led by Herbert S. Hadley, ex-governor of Missouri and then a professor of law at the University.<sup>151</sup>

146. Council Minutes, Sept. 15, 1925. He had been city attorney since 1918, and had been acting manager prior to the appointment of E. O. Heinrich.

The first fight had been a clean one compared to this. The opponents now resorted to charges that the town had no law enforcement, that bootlegging was rife, that a city truck hauled sugar to a mountain still, that a city policeman who was murdered had been put out of the way by the city administration, and that the municipality was poisoning the populace by putting chlorine in the water.<sup>152</sup> Again the defenders of the Charter waged a vigorous campaign,<sup>153</sup> too, not only by publishing facts and figures but also by shouting "lies," and by contending that the Ku Klux Klan was sponsoring the opposition.<sup>154</sup> Again they won, this time by a vote of 1709 to 1284.<sup>155</sup>

In both these contests the Hare system, as well as the manager plan, had been at stake, and afterwards, in 1933, the critics of that method of voting made an issue of it.<sup>156</sup> However the Hare system was sustained by a majority of about 500.<sup>157</sup> An argument against it was that too many electors

156. The News-Herald criticized the system because there were too many spoiled ballots, Nov. 6, 1929.

157. Camera, Nov. 9, 1933; Council minutes, Nov. 7, 1933; The National Municipal League again sent a representative who organized a house to house canvas. "Attacks against P. R. Repulsed in Hamilton and Boulder," National Municipal Review (Dec., 1933), 22:613-14.

spoiled their ballots because they had not learned to mark their choices with numerals instead of an X. Another criticism, not really the fault of the Hare system but of the ruling that candidates should be listed alphabetically, was that from 1917 to 1934 not a single candidate whose initial was in the latter half of the alphabet had been elected. Since then four in that category have won seats on the council, but two of them were appointed for a part of a term before being elected to a full term. An additional problem was that when the system worked well and people were content, too many electors neglected to vote. On the other hand, the arguments for the Hare system were that when a voter's first choice was lost his second choice still might count, that in this way minority interests had some representation on the council, and that the personal choice element in voting made it difficult for a political party to fill a majority of the seats and control the council.<sup>158</sup>

158. Fisher and Starrett, op. cit., pages 88-9; Boulder County Studies, 1920-21, page 154; Don C. Sowers, "Sixteen Years of P. R. in Boulder, Colorado," National Municipal Review (Jan. 1934), 23: 27-30; News-Herald, Nov. 6, 1929; interview with Scott Mitchell, April 19, 1946. "Frequently there have been no noticeable issues in Boulder's municipal elections other than the competence and popularity of the individual candidates." "Boulder's Thirteenth P. R. Election," National Municipal Review (Feb., 1942); 31: 127-8. In 1932 only three Colorado cities had a lower percentage of the electorate casting votes. E. H. Cramer, Survey of General Civic Conditions (Boulder: University of Colorado, 1934), page 21.

An attack on the Hare system was also an attack, in a measure, on the city manager plan and its freedom from party control. Therefore the reaffirmation of the Hare system also attested that the city manager plan had become accepted.<sup>159</sup> By this time most of Boulder's principal streets had been paved, thereby removing that issue,<sup>160</sup> and then and since the popular and astute leadership of F. L. Moorhead, George W. Teal and H. C. McClintock, as managers, and of J. O. Billig, L. W. Cumberford, Dr. H. H. Heuston, and F. W. Thurman,<sup>161</sup> as mayors, has contributed much to the continuation of this form of government. There had been a long, hard struggle, though, before the present system became traditionally established.

When the present plan is compared with the earlier form of government, referring particularly to personnel standards, some noteworthy differences are brought out. Some of them are attributable to the city manager plan while others represent the trends of the times. In the first place, the

people who do the work of the city now are for the most part employees in organized departments, instead of being, as in many cases before, individually elected, independent representatives of the people, or else party wheelhorses.<sup>162</sup> As employees they have fixed responsibilities and hours of work, vacations with pay, compensation insurance, and group life insurance policies.<sup>163</sup> In addition the firemen and policemen have a retirement and disability benefit fund and civil service regulations as to appointment, advancement, and dismissal.<sup>164</sup> The municipal employees were unorganized until recently, but finally in 1946 they formed a local association and started working for salary increases.<sup>165</sup>

Another recent development has been the professionalization of municipal service, which has been fostered by the Colorado Municipal League under the sponsor-

The League makes available to member cities its information services, copies of ordinances, special reports, the League accounting system, a monthly magazine, an in-service training program, a field service representative, participation in annual conventions, and cooperation for legislative action.<sup>166</sup> The city manager and attorney have been active participants in the affairs of the League; the one has been president and the other has been chairman of the attorney's section.<sup>167</sup> The city manager has also been active in the international association of city managers, and the assistant engineer has been chairman of the Rocky Mountain section of the electrical inspectors' organization.<sup>168</sup> In addition the fire and police chiefs both have long been members and have served terms as presidents of their respective regional organizations.<sup>169</sup> The trend towards professionalization has been carried further by participation of Boulder employees in state "schools" for firemen, policemen, and finance officers, and by the training

A longer term of service, on the average, has made possible much of the recent professional interest. An analysis of turnover shows that in all city departments the average was 5.8 changes a year from 1871 to 1918, while from 1918 to 1946 for the corresponding offices the figure was 2.3.<sup>171</sup> Likewise when a list of city officials of long service is prepared, it is loaded heavily with those of recent years.<sup>172</sup> The following list is an "honor roll" of those who have served the city for ten years or more, including a period in some key position, and also three others who were thrice mayor and one who was city manager for five years:<sup>173</sup>

## CHAPTER THREE

### PUBLIC SERVICES

In order for people to live together in cities ways must be found to provide them with certain essential supplies in large quantities, and this in turn calls for adequate transportation and communication facilities. Moreover this is vital to the very commerce which helps make the city. However, here is one field in which competition usually breaks down, because the providing of such services are natural monopolies. It simply is not practical to have competing street car lines or gas companies or water systems, and without competition the citizens fear that they may be overcharged for the services or find them inadequately provided. Therefore the electors call upon their municipal government to provide certain services and to regulate the public utilities companies which supply the others.

In Boulder one fundamental transportation network, the city streets, and one vital supply system, the water works, are publicly owned. Certain other supply systems, as gas and lights, and the principal transportation and communication services, as railways, local buses, and telegraph and telephone systems, come under some regulation.<sup>1</sup> Aviation belongs in a special category.

1. Certain foodstuffs are under surveillance, too, but since this is a health measure, not applying to a public utility, it is treated in the next chapter.

Before the Town of Boulder was first incorporated, in 1871, the county was responsible for the care of the few town streets, which were then in the county system of roads.<sup>2</sup> Consequently part of Pearl Street was "improved" before it came under town supervision,<sup>3</sup> and the first street to be improved extensively by the town was Twelfth.<sup>4</sup> Rights of way for these and some other streets in the original town were transferred by the early Town Company to the county and then by the county to the town.<sup>5</sup> On through the years, as the community spread over more territory, the town, and later the city, bought up the strips of land needed to open new streets and alleys.<sup>6</sup>

In the first set of ordinances adopted in 1871 was one providing that the street commissioner should make a list of all able-bodied men and call on each for two days' labor on the streets each year, or else collect four dollars in lieu

Occasionally someone donated the land for a street, as did Lewis Cheney, ibid., Nov. 20, 1883. When one new addition was to come in in 1891 the property owners demanded streets, water mains, sidewalks, and street lights at once! Camera, June 2, 1891.

of work and use the funds for improvement of the streets.<sup>7</sup> However, keeping the streets in good condition was a difficult, endless task, because of the steep grades and the clay soil.<sup>8</sup> Consequently there were many complaints about their "horrible condition," especially after heavy rains.<sup>9</sup> One disgusted townsman contributed this poem to the newspaper:

Oh the mud, the miserable mud!  
 Into its depths you go with a thud  
 That causes the sticky nasty clay  
 To slide up your boots in a horrible way!  
 Sticky, nasty, filthy stuff!  
 Why, the devil! it's enough  
 To cause oaths to come from pious saints.  
 Everything here is damned with the taints  
 Of the nasty, adhering real estate,  
 That's loafing round your town at date.<sup>10</sup>

Nevertheless some improvements were made in the years 1870 to 1900. In those years sidewalks were built along most of the improved streets, and they were made of "sound two inch plank, firmly set upon and spiked to suitable bearings....".<sup>18</sup> The ordinances provided that property owners should build the walks in front of their places whenever a majority in a block so requested; but if not done the city would build them and assess the cost to property owners.<sup>19</sup> Actually the city built a good many, for property owners often were delinquent in compliance.<sup>20</sup> It was not long until the nails protruded and the boards came loose on these sidewalks, creating a "woeful state of man traps,"<sup>21</sup> which caused many a tumble and several lawsuits.<sup>22</sup> Experiments with stone flagging led to the adoption of stone for Pearl Street in 1886, and finally in 1898 the city council ordered the street supervisor "to tear up all the wooden sidewalks in the city limits."<sup>23</sup>

18. Council Minutes, Dec. 13, 1871.

19. Ibid., and June 1, 1872; Dec. 9, 1875; Oct. 16, 1877; August 14, 1882; Sept. 7, 1888, Nov. and Dec., 1888; March 2, 1891; August 3 and Oct. 5, 1896; et passim.

20. Ibid., Jan. 4, 1873; July 7, 1881, Dec. 4, 1882; March 3, 1884; et passim.

21. Herald, March 3, 1880; Sept. 24, 1884; Camera, June 14 and Sept. 23, 1891. Also, the reminiscences of George W. Teal and W. H. Burger, ibid., Dec. 7, 1928; Feb. 19-23, 1945.

22. Herald, April 23, July 26, 1882; Council Minutes, June 7, 1886. Also see Chapter VI.

23. Council Minutes, August 1, 1898.

Major improvements in both streets and sidewalks were initiated after 1900 and carried out extensively after the first World War. First, crushed stone was tried on a few streets downtown,<sup>24</sup> and some concrete sidewalks were built.<sup>25</sup> Then came a noteworthy development, namely, the organization of "Improvement Districts," starting with No. 1 on Tenth Street from Aurora to University in 1907.<sup>26</sup> By this plan, when a majority of the property holders in a district petitioned for improvements, as macadamized streets with concrete gutters and sidewalks, the city would have the work done by means of a bond issue and then assessed the cost against the property owners over a period of years.<sup>27</sup> Within five years ten such districts had been organized and the work had been completed in seven of them,<sup>28</sup> just in time to prepare Boulder for the reception of a considerable tourist trade.<sup>29</sup>

When the business men became interested in the paving of Pearl Street, they turned naturally, then, to the

1. Report of Engineer and Street Committee.

3. After hearing any objections the Council acts.
4. Ordinance published.
5. Passage of the ordinance.
6. Advertising for bids.
7. Opening and consideration of bids.
8. Letting of the contract.
9. Engineer's statement of approved completion of the work.

11. Publication of notice of completion of work.

Incidentally, as the improvement of the streets was launched it was realized that if ever storm sewers were to be built to carry off heavy rains, they should be laid first; consequently work on that proceeded along with the paving.<sup>33</sup>

In 1919 some alleys downtown were paved by improvement districts.<sup>34</sup> By that time forty blocks of streets were on petition for paving,<sup>35</sup> and when this and additional improvements were undertaken under Scott Mitchell, it provoked the attack on the city manager plan which has been discussed previously.<sup>36</sup> Nevertheless the paving of streets was pushed with little interruption during all the uproar,<sup>37</sup> so that by 1923 ten miles of streets were paved,<sup>38</sup> and by

The building of the water system has been even more difficult than the improving of streets. In fact, keeping this growing community adequately supplied with water has been a headache from start to finish, and it is not finished.

At first the householders obtained their water from the creek and from the irrigation ditches,<sup>49</sup> and later a few residents had water piped from the ditches into their houses.<sup>50</sup> With the rapid growth of the town after 1870, a better water system was needed, especially for protection against fire.<sup>51</sup> Consequently at a "mass meeting" in 1874 a committee was named to draft plans and to consider whether a private company or the town board should build a water system.<sup>52</sup> At the next mass meeting, when the committee submitted a plan, there was considerable sentiment in favor of organizing a stock company to build the water works, but finally the decision was in favor of making this a public enterprise. A petition then was drawn up asking the town board to call for a vote on a bond issue of \$18,000 for this

49. "There is no water running in the farmer's ditch, which should supply the tidy housewives of Boulder, who are grumbling considerably thereat." Pioneer, Feb. 10, 1869.

purpose.<sup>53</sup> After the bonds had been approved by a vote of 71 to 17 on October 31, 1874,<sup>54</sup> the town board let bids for the building of a reservoir near Douty's Mill in Boulder Canon, for laying a small cast iron main down Pearl Street,<sup>55</sup> and for digging a ditch to carry creek water from Hanging Rock to the reservoir.<sup>56</sup>

When this first water system was nearing completion the president of the town board, Ephraim Pound, was assigned an official chore. He was appointed "to canvass the town and find out whom will take water and what they are willing to give per annum or per quarter for the use of water."<sup>57</sup> However, even before the lines were all laid, it became clear that this system was inadequate, consequently another bond issue of \$12,000 was required to complete the ditch and to lay pipes along cross streets.<sup>58</sup> When the work was finished the contractor was required to give bond to "make tight all joints, connections and hydrants now leaking or that may be

The rapid growth of the town soon brought petitions for extensions of the mains.<sup>61</sup> Hence in 1878 the townsmen had to authorize another bond issue for these improvements,<sup>62</sup> but no sooner was this done than the need for another reservoir and larger mains became urgent.<sup>63</sup> Although for Eastern consumption the editor of the Herald boasted that the "water works are unexcelled," in his local column two weeks later he wrote: "The water question is getting to be a serious one," and because the water was clouded with mill tailings, he added that the supply should be taken from the creek farther up the canon.<sup>64</sup>

about \$150,000.<sup>70</sup> It was about time, too, for the water situation was growing steadily worse.<sup>71</sup> A local physician suggested that each home and office be provided with some kind of a water filter,<sup>72</sup> and a university professor recommended to householders a method of treating water with soda and alum solutions, which would "settle 95 per cent. of the dirt in twenty minutes."<sup>73</sup> When the bond issue for the mountain system was approved, 459-39,<sup>74</sup> one enterprising merchant ran an ad that in anticipation of "a large supply of water," he would offer the "greatest bargains in Pure Toilet Soap ever known."<sup>75</sup>

Boulder's first mountain system was built and in operation by March, 1891,<sup>76</sup> but then, as always, more troubles arose. For one, the reservoir had been put into operation without being paved, so that had to be done.<sup>77</sup> While this work was under way, according to L. C. In subsequent years the principal problems have been to tap more sources of supply in order to keep abreast of the city's growth, and to guard the sources against pollution after the mountain region became the nation's playground. To expand the sources the city drew upon the Gregory Canon Ditch in 1902 to fill the first reservoir for the Chautauqua,<sup>86</sup> and in 1904 acquired the Goose Lakes reservoir.<sup>87</sup> Next, after some legal difficulties had been ironed out, in 1906, the city bought of James P. Maxwell his water rights in Silver Lake.<sup>88</sup> Considerably later, in 1935, the city purchased the four Green Lakes, from which it had been obtaining some water

by contract since 1933.<sup>89</sup> This was followed in 1941 by the acquisition of Glacier Lake.<sup>90</sup>

As the city reached back into the mountains for water, and as campers began to penetrate the same region, a water supply which once had been almost 100 per cent. pure showed signs of pollution.<sup>91</sup> Consequently a chlorination plant was installed at the intake near Lakewood.<sup>92</sup> In the meantime the city had discovered that the government would sell its mountain lands for park purposes,<sup>93</sup> and this led to the purchase of Arapahoe Glacier in 1928 in order to control that watershed area. The unique position of Boulder, then, as the owner of a glacier, gave the city considerable publicity.<sup>94</sup>

"They may be found in the shadow of Arapahoe Glacier; along the transmission line from Blue Bird falls to the city reservoirs; in the far extremities of the city and at the outlet of the city sewage."<sup>104</sup>

The building of the water system was expensive. In 1920 the system was valued at about one million dollars, and half of that was not paid for yet.<sup>105</sup> However, under the first city manager heavy water users had been put on meters and the flat rates for other users had been increased for the purpose of making the revenues pay all the expenses of the water department, including the retirement of bonds.<sup>106</sup> Where there had been an annual deficit of over \$20,000 before 1918, there was by 1919 a net profit of about \$34,000.<sup>107</sup> This "pay as you go" basis has since been continued, and by 1938 the city manager anticipated that when the bonds were all retired and a few more projects completed, the water rates might be reduced.<sup>108</sup> However, paying for the water

108. Camera, Feb. 2, 1938. During the war, when improvements could not be made, the accumulated funds were invested in War Bonds. Ibid., Sept. 2, 1942; June 10, 1945; Council Minutes, April 20, 1943. By 1945 only \$119,000 in bonds remained outstanding. Annual Report, page 9.

system is not the only problem. The city continues to draw heavily on the supply, especially in the summer, with the result that in dry seasons there are critical shortages and the sprinkling of lawns has to be curtailed.<sup>109</sup> Consequently the people of this community watch with vital interest the reports about snowfall in the mountains and the amount of reserve in the mountain lakes.<sup>110</sup> That is one thing which even the best-managed city government could not regulate.

A number of other public services have been provided by private corporations working in close relationship with the city. The account which is included here deals merely with that relationship and does not assume at all to be a full history of those utilities.

First in this category were the railways. At about the time the town was organized the county had issued bonds to aid the building of the Denver and Boulder Valley Rail Road and the Colorado Central Rail Road to Boulder.<sup>111</sup> When the Denver and Boulder Valley stopped at Erie from lack of funds, the Denver and Boulder Valley Extension Co. was organized to

buy and grade the right of way from Erie to Boulder.<sup>112</sup> One of the first acts of the town trustees was to call an election for approval of a bond issue of \$10,000 to aid the Extension Co.,<sup>113</sup> and the bonds were authorized by a vote of 154-2 in December, 1871.<sup>114</sup> Then after the line was graded, the Extension Co. sold it to the D. and B. V. proper.<sup>115</sup>

Even with this aid the D. and B. V. failed to complete the line to Boulder for almost two years.<sup>116</sup> The disgust of the townsmen was well expressed by the editor of the News:

"First, subscribe about one half of all you possess to secure the grading and tying, then make an extra contribution to procure the right of way; vote town bonds to raise means for bridging, and if you feel satisfied with the road, allow it to remain in that condition, but if you really want to have it ironed, you must give the remainder of your possessions to purchase the iron. Now turn over the road to Denver parties, and you may be sure they will operate it, if it pays."<sup>117</sup>

112. News, Nov. 10, 1871.

113. Minutes, Nov. 10, 1871.

114. Ibid., Dec. 19, 1871; News, Dec. 22, 1871.

116. The first train arrived, News, Sept. 12, 1873.

117. Ibid., May 2, 1873.

Since the railway had not fulfilled its obligations, the town trustees repudiated the bonds.<sup>118</sup> Because the bonds were then held by innocent parties, suit was brought for their payment.<sup>119</sup> After a long legal battle for which the town employed Willard and Henry M. Teller as special counsel, the state supreme court finally granted a judgement against the town in 1879. As a result the trustees had to put up about \$1500 immediately and then paid the balance, nearly \$14,000 for principal and interest, in installments from 1880 through 1882. To raise this sum the tax levy was increased from six to twelve mills.<sup>120</sup> Thus this first experience with a railway company was a rather costly, unhappy affair.

Later experiences pertained mainly to the granting of franchises. On July 8, 1881, a right of way was granted the Denver, Western and Pacific, and three days later another was approved for the Greeley, Salt Lake and Pacific.<sup>121</sup> On June 7, 1897, permission to build through the city was granted to the proposed Inter-Mountain Railway, and a year later a similar concession was made to the Colorado and North Western.<sup>122</sup> Other actions in those years dealt with the

location of depots,<sup>123</sup> the conformance of tracks to street grades,<sup>124</sup> the sale of water to railroad tanks,<sup>125</sup> consideration of a requirement that railways erect gates and post watchmen at crossings,<sup>126</sup> an arrangement for the city to suspend water mains to railway bridges in the Canon,<sup>127</sup> steps to change the Colorado and Southern tracks from Twelfth Street to University hill by old Gamble Field,<sup>128</sup> and the leasing of C. & S. land for park purposes.<sup>129</sup>

After 1900 the major development was the building of the Denver and Interurban, proposed in 1903 and completed six years later.<sup>130</sup> The original company was taken over by the Colorado and Southern,<sup>131</sup> and a twenty year franchise was obtained in December, 1907, for tracks on Pearl and Twelfth streets.<sup>132</sup> From 1915 to 1917 the Interurban Company sought permission to abandon the Twelfth and Pearl Street line and to come into town on the C & S tracks, but before granting

Finally, after the interurban quit running in 1925, city officials protested to the Public Utilities Commission against the discontinuation of this service, but to no avail.<sup>134</sup> Likewise, similar appeals were unsuccessful in the case of the junking of the "Switzerland Trail," the discontinuance of C & S service to Fort Collins, and the elimination of Union Pacific motor trains, Denver to Boulder.<sup>135</sup> The era of railway preeminence had come and gone.

With the first railway had come a telegraph line,<sup>136</sup> but there was only occasional mention of this in the minutes of the city council<sup>137</sup> until in 1897 when the Western Union Telegraph Company obtained a charter to erect lines in the city.<sup>138</sup> No downtown office was established then; consequently a new right of way was approved in 1910.<sup>139</sup>

Later this company agreed to pay a flat annual fee and also to lay underground lines at the time of the paving of the streets.<sup>140</sup>

A telephone system installed in 1881 served only a few business houses,<sup>141</sup> and the company came into Boulder merely by agreeing not to obstruct the highways.<sup>142</sup> A more definitive franchise for setting poles in streets and alleys was granted in 1893,<sup>143</sup> and as the telephone began to come into its own, there were proposals to establish competing systems.<sup>144</sup> Since these were voted down, and the Colorado Telephone Co. had become part of the Mountain States system, when the franchise came up for renewal the city found itself dealing with an extensive monopoly which was suspected of taking excessive profits.<sup>145</sup> As a result Boulder joined with northern Colorado towns in 1913 for cooperation in action to

Later the company resisted regulation by municipalities in favor of regulation by the Public Utilities Commission, but a state supreme court decision held that charter cities had local jurisdiction over such utilities.<sup>149</sup> Subsequently the telephone company made annual financial reports to the city council, and by this time it was operating one of Boulder's big businesses.<sup>150</sup>

In 1882 the local newspaper headlines were: "Efficacious Electricity--Boulder to be Lighted by It,"<sup>151</sup> but five years passed before a small light plant actually was in operation.<sup>152</sup> This came about with little attention in the council chamber, for a certain S. The street lights proved so desirable that by 1890 the city had twenty-seven of them, which cost about \$200 a month.<sup>156</sup> Then the city was "Splendidly Lighted," and: "When the train comes in from Denver at night these lights will loom up grandly and impress everybody, strangers especially, very favorably with the town.... The security these lights offer at night is also an important element, besides the convenience."<sup>157</sup>

In 1897 a proposal to build a municipal plant came to naught,<sup>161</sup> and soon after an electric street railway<sup>162</sup> was built in 1899 its sponsors proposed also to compete for the city light contract.<sup>163</sup> This company, the Boulder Railway and Utility Co., also was soliciting householders and was said to have promise of 400 users.<sup>164</sup> Then it was reported by L. C. Paddock that the old company was boasting that it would "lower lighting rates to such a point that the new company can't exist." The two companies consolidated and became the Boulder Electric Light and Power Co., with a local attorney, A. A. Reed, as secretary.<sup>167</sup> In 1902 the municipality accepted the offer of the above company to supply lights at \$6.25 a month, each, and obtained an agreement that the street car line to the Chautauqua should be continued even if run at a loss.<sup>168</sup> Thus began the involvement of transportation with lighting contracts and rates, which has continued to the present.<sup>169</sup>

By 1905 the city had acquired an extensive mountain water system,<sup>170</sup> and by that time the activity of the Socialist party in Boulder had revived the issue of municipal ownership of a light plant.<sup>171</sup> This idea received stimulation when twice private companies sought permission to use the city water in the Canon for generation of power and then to restore it to the transmission lines.<sup>172</sup>

Since a contract with the Boulder Electric Light Company would not expire until 1913, the city plant was not to be in operation until that date.<sup>176</sup> This postponed the work to the administration of E. Thenceforth the city's relations with the Northern Colorado Power Co., which later became part of the present Public Service Company system, pertained largely to negotiations over the rate structure at the times set for periodic reconsideration and also at the time of renewal of the franchise for twenty years in 1929.<sup>201</sup> The sentiment for public ownership, not fully subsided, was reflected in agitation off and on for lower rates and for a full investigation of the company's assets and costs as a basis for rate making, but the proponents never could muster a majority vote of the council in support of this.<sup>202</sup> Moreover the company's voluntary offers of several successive rate reductions, made possible by increased consumption and lower production costs, tended to offset criticism.<sup>203</sup> Likewise

By that time a street railway system had also become the property of the Public Service Company only to be abandoned in favor of bus lines. In the early years horse railways had been proposed several times,<sup>215</sup> but none was put into operation until 1891. Then a suspicious city council came to terms with a James A. Howze, who promptly treated the council with a box of cigars and later started laying track on Pearl Street.<sup>216</sup> His company suffered financial difficulties,<sup>217</sup> however, and after operating horse cars for a short time it abandoned the line and the city ordered the tracks covered up.<sup>218</sup>

By 1920, when passenger automobiles were making inroads on street car trade, the company was granted permission to raise the fare from five to ten cents for adults, which reduced the trade even further.<sup>228</sup> Consequently the company asked permission either to abandon the street railway system or else to be exempt from paying its assessments for the paving of the streets which were turning its business to the automobiles, but both were denied and an increase in light rates was granted instead.<sup>229</sup> Thenceforth consideration of light rate revisions inevitably had to take into account the transportation deficit,<sup>230</sup> and this continued to hold true even after the Public Service Company substituted motor buses for street cars in 1931.<sup>231</sup> However, during the years when

war-time gasoline rationing threw a heavy passenger load on the buses, the transportation system for once was operated without a deficit.<sup>232</sup>

Other public carriers have also come under municipal regulations designed to insure safety, reasonable rates, and fair trade practices. Such regulations have been applied to the earlier drays and omnibuses and to the modern taxi cabs and scenic cars.<sup>233</sup> In the case of the latter the city officials became involved in difficulties stemming from a "taxi war" in 1934. After one of the operators defied the city and later got into a fight with a policeman, city officials were in court off and on for five years.<sup>234</sup>

Travel by airplane brought a new and special type of problem. No one had a monopoly of the air,<sup>235</sup> yet Boulder hardly could afford more than one landing field, which brought the latter into the category of a public service. When interest in an airport crystallized in the late 1920's, the citizens proposed that the municipality build and operate it.<sup>236</sup> Then the Chamber of Commerce took up this project,

and in 1935 the city leased Hayden field northeast of the city, with an option to purchase it.<sup>237</sup> Later the land was bought, a hangar was built, and improvements were made as WPA projects.<sup>238</sup> In 1941 the field was leased to the Mountain States Aviation Company,<sup>239</sup> and expansion of the airport has been among the projects recently considered for post-war development.<sup>240</sup>

As various utilities companies were obtaining franchises to operate their monopolies, two imaginative citizens of Boulder came forward with bright ideas. One proposed in 1917 that the city grant him an exclusive right for five years to operate gasoline filling stations under city supervision, with all other stations eliminated from the city.<sup>241</sup> Another in 1918 suggested that one fuel company be granted the privilege of "supplying coal to the citizens of Boulder under municipal control."<sup>242</sup> If these applications had been accepted, the further relations of the city with those "utilities" would have made this chapter longer.

## CHAPTER FOUR FOR SAFETY AND SECURITY

It goes without saying that community living calls for certain safeguards for persons and property. Among these are measures to maintain order, to protect against fire, to combat contagion, and to eliminate hazards. In Boulder, as in other cities the municipal government has been called upon to assume these responsibilities.

The maintenance of order was the first to receive attention.<sup>1</sup> At the initial meeting of the town trustees, November 4, 1871, they appointed Oscar Allen constable,<sup>2</sup> and a week later they adopted ordinances governing that office. The first-mentioned duty was "to suppress riots, disturbances and breaches of the peace, to apprehend all rioters, disorderly persons and disturbers of the public peace," as well as anyone violating a town ordinance.<sup>3</sup>

The full extent of the constable's duties, then, is revealed in the various ordinances concerning misdemeanors.<sup>4</sup> For one thing, he should not permit anyone to appear in a public place "in a state of nudity, or in a dress not belonging to his or her sex, or in any indecent or lewd

1. In the very early years, according to George F. Chase, the community had "club rules." "The club ruled the town. Any misdeeds were punished by our very able vigilance committee." Camera, Nov. 22, 1909. From 1861 to 1871 the town was under the jurisdiction of the county sheriff, and the county built the first local jail. See Chapter Two.

2. Minutes.

3. Ibid., Nov. 11, 1871.

4. Ibid., Nov. 14, 1871.

dress." He was also to apprehend all persons who became intoxicated in any public place or in a private house, and put them in jail until they were sober (but there was no town jail yet!) In addition it was unlawful to fire any cannon, gun, or "squib-cracker" without permission, and also unlawful to throw a stone or missile at any building, tree, or property, in a public place, or to obstruct or damage the streets in any way. Further, no one should ride or drive a horse "inordinately" nor leave horses on the streets without tying, and the use of profane language was a misdemeanor calling for a fine of from five to a hundred dollars.<sup>5</sup>

Constable Allen went to work immediately, judging by the reports of arrests he made in December that year.<sup>6</sup> Apparently the ordinance about profanity was not to be taken lightly, either, for in one case the culprit drew a five dollar fine for "the use of indecent and improper language."<sup>7</sup> By the spring of 1873 the editor of the News was moved to comment that "business in the local courts is remarkably

5. The ordinances on misdemeanors were revised in 1878 to exclude gambling, bawdy houses, prostitution, lewd singing or dancing, immoral publications, lewd plays, loud noises, disturbing religious worship, and indecent exposure. Ordinances, II, page 307.

7. Ibid. However, there was quite a row in town the last week in January when two men got into a fight and shot up the store of F. A. Squires. Ibid., Jan. 31, 1873.

dull."<sup>8</sup> A few years later the News-Courier added that "The criminal record of the town is almost at zero, and it is hoped it will continue so."<sup>9</sup> The good record in this respect was praised by Amos Bixby in his history of the county published in 1880.<sup>10</sup> Consequently all the mayor had to be disturbed about that year was violations of ordinances on "the explosion of firearms, and fast driving, and the running at large of cattle and poultry."<sup>11</sup>

As to gambling, there was a report in 1873 that a keno establishment had started and was "running in full blast every evening."<sup>12</sup> Promptly the town trustees adopted an ordinance which read:

"If any person shall sit up, have or keep any keno table, Faro bank, Shuffle board, Vagtell playing cards or other instrument, device or thing whatever wheron or which any money or other article shall in any manner be played for, or if any person shall play for money or any valuable thing

at any game with cards, dice or with any article, device or thing whatever which may be used for the purpose of playing or betting upon or winning or losing money or other property, or shall bet on any game others may be playing, any person so offending shall on conviction be fined in any sum not less than five nor more than fifty dollars."<sup>13</sup>

Soon the newspaper reported that "There has never been any alarming amount of gambling in Boulder, but the advent of a keno establishment in town has awakened our town authorities. The keno establishment has been closed, owing to a lack of support."<sup>14</sup> Nevertheless a few years later gambling again became a matter of concern,<sup>15</sup> and in 1892, after some arrested gamblers claimed that they had not received "fair play," because they had already paid some fines, the Camera commented: "The question is, shall the gambling be allowed under police surveillance or shall it be secret. It is bound to exist one way or the other as long as the state of society is as it is."<sup>16</sup>

The problem of prostitution also received attention from time to time. Although an ordinance of 1873 outlawed "Bawdy houses,"<sup>17</sup> a few years later there was news about the

He wrote: "Public sentiment, be it said, to the disgrace of the place, will not uphold the council in such a course.... A mistaken idea has gone abroad that that class of people brought trade to the place, when the fact is they merely follow in the wake of it."<sup>19</sup> To this he added "Let them be suppressed. If this is impossible, let them be driven away from the most prominent places in the city."<sup>20</sup> The evil was not promptly suppressed, and in the 1890's Boulder had an established "red light district."<sup>21</sup>

Down through the years some sort of a jail had been provided for the confinement of offenders. Although in 1873 the town had ordered a "calaboose" built,<sup>28</sup> the next year the trustees were trying to get the use of the jail in the county court house.<sup>29</sup> A year later Anthony Arnett was paid \$136 for some kind of a calaboose,<sup>30</sup> but soon, after the town acquired a building at Twelfth and Front streets for the fire department,<sup>31</sup> the basement rooms were used for a jail.<sup>32</sup> In 1881 somewhat better quarters were obtained by renting the basement rooms in the county jail building.<sup>33</sup> After some difficulties arose in 1890 concerning the further use of the

In the meantime steps had been taken to make an organized police department of the city's enforcement agencies. Prior to 1882 police cases had been taken before the local justices of the peace.<sup>40</sup> With reincorporation as a city, in 1882 the office of police magistrate was created,<sup>41</sup> and in 1889 an ordinance was adopted formally establishing the police magistrate's court.<sup>42</sup> Before this court offenders

The next step towards improved organization came in 1901 when, under Marshal L. P. Bass, rules and regulations were drawn up for the police department. These prohibited drinking or visiting saloons, and governed the uniform and general conduct on duty.<sup>44</sup> By 1912 the Marshal had a motorcycle,<sup>45</sup> and later other motorcycles<sup>46</sup> and then police cars were employed for patrolling the city.<sup>47</sup> By that time, under the Charter, the office of marshal had become that of chief of police,<sup>48</sup> with from six to eight men in the department.<sup>49</sup> Under Chief C. Back at the turn of the century the major problems had been thievery, gambling, and prostitution. Shortly, however, a general civic campaign had aided the police department in its efforts to combat these.<sup>53</sup> Boulder then settled down for a while to quietude and temperance, and for this much credit was given to Marshal L. P. Bass, whose "tact and quiet forcefulness" made him the "leader of a law-abiding element."<sup>54</sup> During the period of the first World War there was a scourge of hold-ups and burglaries, until stamped out by the police,<sup>55</sup> and in the 1920's bootleggers, drunken

courtesy.

By this time, too, the city division for fighting fires was well organized and well equipped, and had an interesting history extending back through seventy-five years. The first protection against fire was provided by the appointment of fire wardens, something like the recent organization of air-raid wardens under Civilian Defense during World War II. One of the first ordinances to which the first town trustees gave their attention outlined several precautionary regulations concerning fireplaces, stoves, chimneys, lamps, candles, hay, and ashes, and then provided that the constable and trustees should be ex-officio fire wardens. It was their duty to inspect all buildings and to report any violations of the rules to the justice of the peace. At a fire all citizens present were to be subject to the orders of these wardens and if someone refused to obey he was to be fined five dollars and "taken into custody until the fire was over."<sup>62</sup>

Not entirely satisfied with this plan, the trustees two days later employed Oscar Allen, the constable, as a special fire warden at twenty-five dollars a month.<sup>63</sup> Next, in response to the clamor for more effective steps, <sup>64</sup> the board

concluded in 1874 "to ascertain the probable cost of getting hooks and ladders sufficient for a fire company." This was followed by a meeting in February at which eleven men signed up as the charter members of the company.<sup>66</sup> Immediately the town bought a hook and ladder truck from the Denver fire department,<sup>67</sup> and before February had passed the Phoenix Hook and Ladder Company had appeared in uniform on its first parade and had sponsored its first dance.<sup>68</sup>

The Boulder and Macky hose companies participated in many firemen's tournaments from 1877 to 1901, and both put forward championship teams. Boulder Hose led off by winning a silver urn and tray at the Georgetown competition in August, 1877,<sup>80</sup> and sparked by George and D. C. Fonda the team won consistently in other contests, including the final one at the Quarto-Centennial celebration at Boulder in 1901.<sup>81</sup> They were credited with a world's record of 30 2/5 seconds for the run in the "wet hose test" at Canon City in 1896,<sup>82</sup> and the Macky team, also speeded by the dexterity of the two Fondas,<sup>83</sup> established a world's record of 29.1

The fire companies had more important business, though, than their tournaments and dances and parades. They were organized to fight fire, and this they did well, too. After one of the first fires at which the Boulder Hose company functioned, the editor of the newspaper commended the "gallant hose boys" and thanked providence that Boulder had a fire department and a water works.<sup>86</sup> Many times after that there were reports about the companies racing to fires,<sup>87</sup> whereupon the "flames subsided like magic."<sup>88</sup> The long list of buildings saved from destruction includes, among other

On the other hand, the "boys" sometimes encountered unexpected difficulties. One incident was reported as follows:

"Last Friday afternoon, as the thermometer indicated several degrees below zero, the alarm of fire was given. It soon brought a crowd on the street, but when the location was known, most of the people crowded near to their warm stoves and let the fire laddies go alone. The fire was a smoke house on the south side of the creek and was an entire loss, as the hydrant near by was out of order and no water could be thrown. Both hose companies were out, but Boulder Hose, having taken the short cut gained the plug. The Mackys very sensibly hitched on to an express wagon, and returned from the scene not near so worn out and exhausted as the Boulder Hose."<sup>91</sup> Then, too, there was that bad fire at Storey's warehouse, when the hose companies found the water pressure too low to be effective,<sup>92</sup> and another occasion when the

The main hall, 28' x 36', was for city council meetings. At the rear was a bell tower 60 feet high. Herald, Nov. 23, 1881. Later \$166 was paid for a fire bell. Council Minutes, Sept. 5, 1882.

on Twelfth, and the Mackys used it for a headquarters instead of the new building on Fourteenth.<sup>97</sup> The city also purchased equipment for the fire companies, including fire bells and a new hook and ladder truck.<sup>98</sup> All the time more fire hydrants were being installed in the water system,<sup>99</sup> and in 1897 a Gamewell fire alarm system with twelve alarm boxes was put into operation.<sup>100</sup>

Down to that time the city fire department was still on a volunteer basis. In fact the tradition of a democratically managed volunteer department had become so well established that the change to administrative control by the city council came slowly. The first two fire companies had been organized before the town trustees adopted an ordinance providing for that step, hence when the ordinance was passed, in 1876, it provided that the volunteer companies should make such rules

This procedure, once established, became firmly rooted. Year after year the volunteers assembled at a joint annual meeting for the election of officers;<sup>104</sup> sometimes the decision was made easily and sometimes it came after a spirited contest.<sup>105</sup> In 1878 the volunteers expressed "their disapproval of any attempt at what is understood as jobbery

Finally, another recent development of interest is the extension of the work of the firemen into a number of "extra-curricular" activities. They inspect buildings, engage in fire prevention campaigns, take part in parades, renovate their equipment, stand guard at large public gatherings, charge fire extinguishers in public buildings, erect flags, banners and signs, put up Christmas decorations, sponsor Christmas and relief projects, and hold their annual masquerade balls for the benefit of their pension fund.<sup>149</sup> Altogether they serve the city in many ways, while also maintaining the constant vigil required by their main responsibility.

Closely related to the work of the fire department is the regulation and inspection of buildings. The first ordinances, adopted in 1871, provided that the fire wardens should inspect buildings, construction work, manufacturing plants, and ash depositories, and also, from time to time, "inspect all stoves, hearths, fireplaces, grates, furnaces, boilers, and all stove pipes." In addition they required that no hay or straw should be burned near any buildings, that ashes should be placed in covered vaults or else in the middle of the street, and that "no lighted candle or lamp shall be used in any stable or other place or building where hay or straw or other like combustible material may be kept...."<sup>150</sup> These regulations were followed soon by others, establishing "fire limits," or a district in which no frame buildings should be erected,<sup>151</sup> banning the storing of explosives in the town,<sup>152</sup> and requiring that all builders apply to the town surveyor before erecting a building.<sup>153</sup>

In 1906 the city council adopted an ordinance establishing the office of building inspector and outlining his duties,<sup>154</sup> but after the courts declared it invalid

because not authorized by state law,<sup>155</sup> this had to be done over again in 1911,<sup>156</sup> followed by revisions in 1921.<sup>157</sup>

The various regulations governing building, supplemented in places by certain nationally approved measures, were compiled in detail in Chapter IV of Charter and Code of Boulder, Colorado, 1925.<sup>158</sup> This, along with subsequent revisions, is the guide followed by the building inspector, who in 1938, for example, held many consultations with builders and repairmen, investigated complaints about violations, issued 207 building permits and examined that construction work, and together with the fire chief made 166 inspections of business buildings to point out hazards.<sup>159</sup> In 1946, because of the housing emergency and innovations in construction methods, the city council established an appeal board to pass upon special requests for permission to deviate from the requirements of the standard codes.<sup>160</sup>

To implement the building code, the city also required plumbers and electricians to be licensed; Council Minutes, Dec. 17, 1902; June 1, 1904; March 21, 1922; et passim.

Certain other regulations have been adopted to ensure safety. These have included measures requiring the inspection of kerosene used in lamps,<sup>161</sup> banning the promiscuous use of fireworks,<sup>162</sup> bringing open ditches under city surveillance,<sup>163</sup> controlling the practices of beauty parlors (prior to state licensing),<sup>164</sup> and, above all, regulating automobile traffic.<sup>165</sup>

Civic security also called for public health measures. Back in 1875 the town trustees ruled out the keeping of cattle, sheep, or swine, if offensive, and ordered that no privy or open sewer should be allowed to become "nauseous, foul, offensive, or injurious to the public health."<sup>166</sup> Next, in 1877, a state law authorized the setting up of town health boards which, among other things, should keep a record

G. Chase, was given a special order to take all steps necessary to combat a small pox epidemic in 1879.<sup>170</sup> Thenceforth sometimes all the facilities at command were taxed to the limit by serious epidemics--small pox again in 1888-1889, and 1901; diphtheria in 1890 and 1923; scarlet fever in 1897, 1901, and 1927; influenza in 1918 and 1920; typhoid in 1922; and measles in 1930 and 1934.<sup>171</sup>

refuse is allowed to remain in the ditches and gulleys, no vegetable or animal water is allowed to remain in a decaying condition within the city limits. Every street, every alley, every open lot of ground has been cleaned, and the rubbish hauled away....Boulder has perhaps never before, had such a thorough cleaning...."172

Nevertheless, the town would not stay clean, and a few years later: "Resolved, that it is the sense of the Council that the sanitary condition of Boulder is in a fearful state; the ditches, alleys, and drains are in a filthy condition; infectious diseases are known to be prevalent, and in the opinion of the Council absolute quarantine in several cases should be maintained."173 In 1895, Dr. D. W. King reported that the closets and ditches were filthy, but that there could be no relief until the city could get sewers.174

east of town. There the sewage went into a settling basin, which drained into the creek.<sup>187</sup>

Next the disposal of the sewage became a problem. After ranchers east of town had complained about the pollution of the creek,<sup>188</sup> the city consulted engineers in 1908 and 1909 and 1921 about what could be done.<sup>189</sup> Nothing was done, however, until this was taken up during the depression of the 1930's,<sup>190</sup> when a modern sewage disposal plant was built at the outlet on the site of the former settling grounds.<sup>191</sup>

The disposal of sewage, however, did not take care of all waste. In the 1870's, after property owners had been warned to "clean up their back lots and alleys,"<sup>192</sup> the town had to do much of that,<sup>193</sup> including the removal of a dead

cow and some dead cats and chickens.<sup>194</sup> Even so, in 1878 the editor of the News wrote "Look at the garbage in the main street of the town. Old boots, trunk covers, boxes, green watermelons, decayed fruits, etc., are scattered in all directions. Stagnant water and mud are too common. The town enjoyed a thorough cleansing in 1881, followed by an inspection by the board of trustees;<sup>196</sup> but two years later it was again in a "filthy condition."<sup>197</sup> From 1886 on the city had an official "scavenger,"<sup>198</sup> and after 1892 a dumping ground was provided.<sup>199</sup> Ten years later the duties of the scavenger, as the city's clean-up man and supervisor

After disposal of sewage, garbage, and trash had been provided for, the city health department turned to other activities. By an ordinance adopted in 1902 the department was to have an advisory board comprised of the mayor and health officer ex officio, two physicians named by the city

of 189 complaints about nuisances.<sup>242</sup> These are among the measures taken for the security of local residents.

Also presumably belonging in this category of regulations for safety and security are the restrictions placed upon the city's bovine and canine population. In the 1880's there was some trouble about cattle running at large,<sup>243</sup> but that was inconsequential compared to the perennial dog problem. Before the town was organized one citizen complained that "there is not another small town in the world that can support as many lazy, useless, cussed dogs as Boulder. Oh, for a municipal government so that we can have a poisonous, killing, exterminating dog law."<sup>244</sup> Probably he was satisfied when one of the first ordinances adopted in 1871 sought to abate the dog nuisance. It provided for licensing and tagging of dogs and for the elimination of all those not thus identified, and the constable was to be paid one dollar for each dog killed.<sup>245</sup> However the treasurer's reports for the 1870's reveal that few dog licenses were sold,<sup>246</sup> and in 1883 petitioners were asking the city council "to have some of the dogs of the city

242. Annual Report, pages 10-11.

243. A steer impounded and sold then went to its original home, Council Minutes, Nov. 5, 1883; cow ordinance repealed, Herald, Jan. 4, 1888; complaint about cattle impounded anyway, Minutes, Feb. 4, 1889; ordinance for impounding, ibid., June 19, 1911.

244. News, May 20, 1871.

245. Town Minutes, Nov. 13, 1875. Repeated in an ordinance of 1875, ibid., June 23, 1875.

246. Ibid., April 6, 1872; April 3, 1877; et passim.

killed off."<sup>247</sup>

Shortly after the turn of the century a vigorous dog-catcher was employed; consequently by 1912 taxes were being paid on 119 dogs and stray ones were rare.<sup>248</sup> Nevertheless in a few years dogs were again regarded by some as a dangerous nuisance, which led the city council to consider an ordinance in 1924 requiring that all dogs should be kept at home, chained up.<sup>249</sup> The Camera then protested that "The trouble in this country is the mania for regulating everything and everybody," and after the proposed ordinance was killed, the editor responded with "Fido has his freedom."<sup>250</sup> Finally, as recently as 1944, the city manager reported to the council that Boulder was "being over-run with nuisances, such as horses, dogs, and other live stock,"<sup>251</sup> but no further restrictions were then adopted.

Indeed, community living had resulted in considerable regulation of "everything and everybody," required for the safety and security of all.

249. Camera, March 5, 1924.

251. Council Minutes, August 15, 1944. In 1941, 906 dogs were licensed, and in 1942, 902. Camera, Dec. 30, 1942. And in 1928 the police had killed 170 dogs and 55 cats, ibid., Dec. 28, 1928.



## CHAPTER FIVE FOR THE GENERAL WELFARE

There was a time when the agencies of government in this country were expected to abstain from direct action for the uplift of the citizenry. The county poor farm would take care of the few paupers, and a government which had some policing authority and some checks on those ambitious for power was regarded as sufficient to provide an atmosphere in which the citizens could thrive by their own individual enterprise and self-discipline. At least that was the theory, even though never rigidly followed, and as time passed it yielded more and more to an opposing theory, that government should take positive action for the welfare of its constituency. The agencies of government were called upon to assume more and more welfare projects, for education, for relief, for employment, for economic security, for the care of the handicapped, for recreation, for encouragement of arts, and so on, until recently this came into full bloom in a veritable era of "welfare government." However the old theory still persists to challenge these developments at every turn, thereby creating some confusion as to just how far a government should go into the field of welfare work.

Local governments, those closest to the people, were the first to respond with welfare legislation and activities, and in spite of some opposition they have responded in far more ways than generally is realized. Boulder is no

exception; in fact in some fields this city has had a record of leadership.

The problem of relief for the poor and for the unemployed was the first to receive attention. Boulder, a growing frontier community, did not feel seriously the panics of 1873 and 1883,<sup>1</sup> but that of '93 created conditions which called for the organization of a "Benevolent Association."<sup>2</sup> That private organization solicited aid to supplement the usual county poor fund, and the two carried the burden of emergency relief. Nevertheless, when a city bond issue for the water works was under consideration, it was expected to "do much to relieve the present hard times."<sup>3</sup> Even so the hard times continued for several years;<sup>4</sup> consequently in 1898 a delegation "appeared before the Council representing the Charity Association formed from the various Churches of the city, and asked the cooperation of the city in providing public work of some kind for destitute but worthy persons out of employment."<sup>5</sup> This was referred to the committee on

2. Comprised of the pastors, the mayor, and others, to aid 100 people who lacked the "necessities of life." Camera, Oct. 10, 1893.

3. Ibid., August 9, 1893.

4. In 1896 Mayor Crockett Ricketts discovered a destitute family and the newspaper solicited help. Ibid., Jan. 17, 1896; the ladies put on a benefit cantata, ibid., April 10, 1896.

streets, and a month later a plan had been prepared, as follows:

"A communication was read from the Christian Relief Association of Boulder asking first, that the city provide a yard where stone may be on hand to be broken up for the use of the streets of the city, and second, that the labor of any persons sent to said yard shall be under the control of someone to be appointed by the city, and third, that the person so appointed shall endorse all orders given to laborers for work done, before such orders shall be valid for meals or lodging, upon the restaurants working with this Association."<sup>6</sup> The council voted acceptance of this proposal, thereby establishing an early precedent for municipally sponsored work relief.<sup>7</sup>

In 1901 another precedent was established when the city council voted "that \$50.00 be appropriated for the relief of the poor of the city, to be expended through the Relief Committee of the Associated Charities."<sup>9</sup> An appeal for more money in 1912 brought forth the recommendation "that the

Associated Charities be requested to canvass their other sources of income at this time, and that the City Council make an appropriation later in the winter, when the need will no doubt be most urgent, and when we will be better able to estimate the demands upon the small contingent fund."<sup>10</sup>

In the meantime the city had continued the policy of pushing construction work at times when it would relieve unemployment.<sup>11</sup> On the other hand, when A. J. Macky left \$50,000 to build a hospital "for the comfort of poor widows and orphan children while sick and unable to care for themselves....provided the City of Boulder....or the County Commissioners...will support and maintain the same...." the city council concluded that it would be "inexpedient" for the city to accept.<sup>12</sup>

Minutes, Feb. 3, 1908. A council committee reported 40 idle teams and 50 idle men. Camera, Feb. 18, 1908.

In 1918 the city took over the visiting nurse service initiated by the Woman's Club,<sup>13</sup> and also under the Charter set up a welfare department with a director who was empowered to "provide for the study of and researches into causes of poverty, delinquency, crime and other social problems in the community."<sup>14</sup> Consequently when the Associated Charities appealed for the usual appropriation, then \$200.00, the city manager replied, "a certain sum had been appropriated to the Public Welfare Department to assist along certain lines similar to the activities of the Associated Charities." He asked that this be brought up later "in order not to disturb the appropriation ordinance now under consideration."<sup>15</sup>

Although the visiting nurse was employed in the city health department, much of her time was given to the examining of school children and the calling on patients in needy homes, which could be classed as "social work."<sup>16</sup> Therefore in the early 1920's she had to struggle against some opposition based upon the contentions that other cities did not employ nurses and that the schools had not asked to

Then came the depression of the 1930's, which created so much unemployment that the county and the Community Chest were over-burdened.<sup>19</sup> In 1931 the city manager developed a plan for the city to sponsor work relief, "rather than to hand out doles."<sup>20</sup> The welfare director, Rudolph Johnson, headed a work relief committee which solicited contributions for the employment of men for improvement of the city parks and the public school grounds and on other projects.<sup>21</sup> In 1932 Boulder received commendation in the Engineering News-Record "for working out such an intelligent relief program, in which the city got value received, and accomplished results of permanent value to the city and to the poor."<sup>22</sup>

As a result of this foresight Boulder had ready plans for other improvements and made the transition quickly to
