H. Heuston proposed that the city council set up a "commission on charity and relief" to centralize the administration of all public and private relief work,<sup>27</sup> the council shied away from it lest it appear that the city was assuming full responsibility. The majority sentiment was that the city government lacked the resources necessary to do that and therefore should "keep out."<sup>28</sup>

By virtue of federal aid, then, Boulder like other cities survived the depression and probably received more permanent benefits in return than did most cities. When the recent war created an artificial prosperity, the full responsibility for relief and social work fell again to the principal agencies of earlier years, the county and the Community Chest, with the exception that the city continued the visiting nurse service. In 1934, during the worst of the depression, the nurse had cared for 332 families and had reported a total of 3,884 calls or visits.<sup>29</sup> In 1943, during the war, she made 1,725 general nursing visits,<sup>30</sup> and in addition did other work, like caring for the health needs of the children at the Day Nursery.<sup>31</sup> The city's relief activities, never involving much besides planning and coordination, and never drawing much on municipal funds, had once more returned to the pre-depression minimum.

Another movement for the sake of the general welfare came with the crystallization of sentiment for local

31. The Day Nursery building, erected in 1930 on part of Barker Park by arrangement with the city, was built by the Lions Club and other contributors and is not municipally owned. prohibition in the latter part of the past century. When Boulder City became an organized town one of the first ordinances assessed a license fee of twenty-five dollars quarterly for selling "spirituous, vinous, fermented and intoxicating liquors."<sup>32</sup> This was done in part to establish control over the saloons,<sup>33</sup> which after 1875 were required to close at midnight and to refrain from employing any "lewd woman."<sup>34</sup> Licensing also was counted upon to supply revenue, and in 1876 liquor stores and billiard halls together paid \$2,425.95 into the town treasury, whereas property owners paid only \$1,778.16 in taxes.<sup>35</sup>

By this time a local temperance movement, at first encouraged by the Good Templars, had become more vocal with the organization of the Prohibition Alliance in 1879.<sup>36</sup> In

32. Town Minutes, Nov. 13, 1871. Previously the county had licensed the saloons. Minutes of the County Commissioners, Jan. 28, 1862; Jan. 10, 1863; Jan. 11, 1864; et passim.

33. They had to give bond in the amount of \$300 to keep all ordinances. Town Minutes, Nov. 13, 1871. The News on Dec. 8, 1871 carried an official notice "that unless the parties selling liquor contrary to the ordinances of the Town of Boulder immediately take out a license they will be prosecuted."

Also the Independent Champions of the Red Cross, News, Jan. 7, 1876, and the Blue Ribbon movement, reported in Denver Daily Times, Sept. 15, and 28, 1877. As early as 1869 over 100 Boulder women sent a contribution of \$93.50 to the local newspaper for "refusing to advertising for liquor sellers." Pioneer, April 7, 1869.

1883 after an ordinance closing saloons on Sundays had been passed only to be repealed in response to the protest of the saloon-keepers, that and other difficulties caused the mayor to resign, whereupon the city council appointed a "dry" mayor pro tempore and again ordered the saloons to close on Sundays.<sup>37</sup> At a meeting a year later the saloon-keepers on the one hand petitioned for a lower license fee, without success, while on the other hand an alderman complained that "at the Board of Trade saloon music was almost constantly going contrary to their agreement." This was ordered stopped or it would cause the license to be revoked.<sup>38</sup> Obviously in those years the city council was between two fires.

Soon, however, prohibition became a campaign issue, and after the "high license" ticket defeated the prohibitionists by a narrow margin in 1885,<sup>39</sup> a majority of the aldermen stood for many years squarely and bluntly in favor of high license fees as a kind of compromise measure. At first J. L. Church, resolute prohibitionist alderman, blocked the adoption of a high license ordinance by refusing to cast his vote against it. The ordinance lacked a majority and with

38. Ibid., Dec. 1, 1884.

39. Chauncey Stokes was the Prohibition candidate defeated by C. C. Brace, who stood for high licenses. Herald, March 18, 25, and April 8, 1885.

his vote there would have been a tie, which would have permitted the mayor to cast the decisive favorable ballot.<sup>40</sup> Later an ordinance raising the license fee to \$1,000 a year carried anyway, and then the number of saloons decreased from fourteen in 1885 to six in 1888.<sup>41</sup>

The city did adopt a rule against selling liquor to minors, but enforcement of this and of the Sunday closing ordinance was reported lax.<sup>42</sup> In 1894 a committee of temperance people appeared before the high license council to ask for a popular vote on whether saloons should be licensed at all, but to no avail.<sup>43</sup> Two years later the petition was presented again,<sup>44</sup> and this time the aldermen devised a clever plan. First they adopted a resolution which read, in part: "in city government it is a question of public policy touching the matter of intoxicating liquors whether the sale of such shall be wholly prohibited with the prospective result of the secret and unlicensed sale of the same by an unlimited number of persons without return to the public

44. Ibid., March 2, 1896.

revenue, or whether such sale shall be open and licensed and restricted to a limited number of persons with returns to the public revenue...."<sup>45</sup> Then they approved having the electorate cast separate ballots on the saloon question, "for the purpose of whatever value there may be in the information," but the result was not to be binding upon the city council because Colorado then had no law providing for local option.<sup>46</sup> At the election that April the vote was 621 to 534 in favor of continued licensing of saloons.<sup>47</sup>

For several years, although the aldermen did limit the number of saloons to ten,<sup>48</sup> they also flaunted their victory at the polls by granting licenses for saloons in blocks where the majority of property owners had protested vigorously.<sup>49</sup> When Isaac T. Earl, prohibitionist, introduced an ordinance forbidding saloons to have any back rooms or anything over the front door "to screen the occupants from the public gaze," the council tabled it for almost a year before bringing it openly to a 5-2 defeat.<sup>50</sup> Finally in 1907 the prohibitionists, stimulated by two successive state conventions of the Women's Christian Temperance Union held at

45. Ibid., March 9, 1896.

46. Ibid.

47. Ibid., April 20, 1896.

48. Ibid., June 7, 1899.

49. Ibid., May 1, 1898; May 3, 1899; March 15, 17, 1905.

50. Ibid., August 2, 1897, to Feb. 7, 1898; Camera, August 3, 1897.

Boulder,<sup>51</sup> again asked the city council to stop licensing saloons, and this time the majority of the aldermen bluntly denied the request on the ground that they had to stand by their high license campaign pledges.<sup>52</sup>

On the very next day the prohibitionists nominated Isaac T. Earl as their candidate for mayor<sup>53</sup> and organized the campaign which led to victory that April, 1907. Although the Camera opposed prohibition as an "unworkable" theory which had failed elsewhere and which would retard the growth of Boulder,<sup>54</sup> L. C. Paddock gracefully acknowledged defeat. He reported that the women workers had been up all night, had gone to church at six o'clock for prayers and coffee, and then had appeared at the polling places at 7:00 A.M. "Woman suffrage, if on trial, had proved itself an institution," he wrote, and then he concluded: "A splendid organization and an almost fanatical zeal rendered it impossible for the non-partisan (high license) organization to make the slightest headway."<sup>55</sup>

As the next election approached, Mayor Earl warned that "the liquor issue is not settled and will not be for years to come."<sup>60</sup> It was at this time that a movement arose for "business management" in municipal affairs, with "no backward step on moral or other lines."<sup>61</sup> The Camera supported this, and L. "A touch of the old Pilgrim spirit is a good thing in any community, that is to say a little of it, just enough to give it a flavor like unto a few drops of vanilla in a barrel of ice cream.

"But let that spirit dominate, and the result is as deadly as the torrid winds that sweep the Sahara, withering all things with its fiery touch."<sup>62</sup>

Although in the election that April, 1909, the Better Boulder party failed to elect their candidates, they lost by a very narrow margin,<sup>63</sup> and they did win strong support for a special resolution confirming that Boulder was "anti-saloon territory."<sup>64</sup> The temperance forces, consolidated considerably by the success of the Billy Sunday revival meetings in 1909,<sup>65</sup> rallied again in 1911 to return the Better Boulder party to office, with a decisive "dry" vote in all four wards,<sup>66</sup> and a proposed repeal of the anti-saloon

The temperance victory finally was so decisive that the next city council, even though not of the reform party, went so far as to ban liquor advertisements on billboards,<sup>68</sup> and when someone moved that the old Crystal Springs brewery be burned, Mayor W. L. Armstrong was reported to have suggested that the motion might well be amended to include all other breweries in the state.<sup>69</sup> Presently, when Colorado went dry in 1916, the city council pledged support<sup>70</sup> and the next year, with the adoption of the Charter, approval was thereby voted for a clause which read: "No license shall ever be granted for the manufacture or sale of intoxicating liquor within the city limits of the City of Boulder, or within its territorial jurisdiction."<sup>71</sup> Since then there have been some attempts to break this rigid rule, but in all such cases Boulder has consistently "stayed on the water wagon."<sup>72</sup>

Somewhat akin to the prohibition movement was the contemporaneous agitation for a curfew and for restrictions

In 1889 the Herald suggested that "Boys should be kept at home after eight o'clock at night unless sent up town on a special errand by their parents."<sup>73</sup> That June the city council, without adopting an ordinance, authorized the police to order all boys under sixteen years of age off the streets after 8:00 P.M.<sup>74</sup> Later, in 1896, the Camera sponsored a curfew for a while, with official approval but no formal ordinance.<sup>75</sup> The next year the city took up this by the adoption of an ordinance which provided that the fire bell should be rung at 8:00 P.M. as a warning for children of ages ten to sixteen to go to their homes.<sup>76</sup> A revision in 1907 entitled "An Ordinance to Encourage Public Order, to Preserve and Safeguard the Health and Morals of Children of Tender Years and to Restrain and Punish Juvenile Delinquents," fixed the curfew hours at 9:00 P.M. to 4 A.M. in the summer and 8:00 P.M. to 6:00 A.M. during the school year.<sup>77</sup> For years enforcement of this was lax,<sup>78</sup> and in 1941, with a state law also applicable, the curfew was invoked in an effort to check the juvenile delinquency then

There have been some other regulations similar to the curfew. One was an ordinance "to prevent theatrical performances, dramatic entertainments, vaudeville exhibitions, baseball games and other similar exhibitions and sports" on Sundays, adopted in 1906,<sup>80</sup> but repealed by a general referendum in 1929,<sup>81</sup> even though the W.C.T.U., the Law Observance League, and the Ministerial Alliance urged continuation.<sup>82</sup> During the recent war, because so many Navy men were at the University, and recreational activities were restricted by gasoline rationing, the bars were lowered further by permitting pool halls, skating rinks, bowling alleys, and the like to open Sundays from 1:00 P.M. to midnight.<sup>83</sup> Once there also had been agitation for the censoring of motion pictures shown in Boulder, but this was dropped following nation-wide efforts for improved standards.<sup>84</sup> In 1922, however, the city welfare department

did take steps to bar certain "immoral magazines" from sale at local news stands.<sup>85</sup> These examples, not all-inclusive, are indicative of the type of legislation employed from time to time in an effort to improve moral standards.

In contrast to the negative effect of restrictions is positive action for the providing of counter-attractions. Quite early this tendency found expression in efforts to maintain some kind of a reading room. As early as 1869 the newspaper directory listed a reading room in the upper front room of the Pioneer building.<sup>86</sup> Shortly after the Boulder County Pioneer was closed out,<sup>87</sup> the following announcement appeared in its successor, the Boulder County News: "An effort will be made to start up the Reading Room for the next six months. Our citizens will be waited upon for subscriptions. Season tickets for gents, \$3; ladies, \$1."<sup>88</sup>

For several years afterward the reading room was frequently being started again by someone or other. In 1875 the Y.M.C.A. sponsored one,<sup>89</sup> and in 1882 the "ladies of

In 1886 a Boulder Library Association was organized with mostly men on the board of trustees,<sup>93</sup> and that year the Board of Trade lent recognition by appointing a committee to work for a public library.<sup>94</sup> The Library Association tried to raise funds by means of a series of lectures, but after the poor attendance at the first one this plan was given

up.<sup>95</sup> The editor of the Herald then wisely remarked:

"The failure of the lecture plan of raising money for a free reading room is not very complimentary to Boulder, and yet it is not much of a surprise to those posted on such matters. Lectures are nowhere very popular. People are so constituted as to want something light, if even trivial, to entertain them when the affair is public."<sup>96</sup>

In 1891 the superintendent of schools proposed to open a reading room if others in the community would help. He suggested that they have "one general, large room, a smoking room, and a place for games without gambling,"<sup>97</sup> but later issues of the newspapers showed no signs of response to his proposal. Finally in 1895 the ladies of the W.C.T.U. enlisted those of the Fortnightly Club and other groups to open a reading room which later became the Boulder Public Library.<sup>98</sup>

A campaign for funds in March and April, 1895, obtained pledges amounting to \$600; consequently at a meeting on April 22, with Mrs. Fred White presiding, a board of managers was elected and authorized to open the reading room.<sup>99</sup> A

constitution adopted a month later provided that the association should have capital stock of \$600 in shares of ten dollars each, with no dividends nor assessments.<sup>100</sup>

The first location was in a room across from the court house, and the first librarian was Mrs. C. H. Savory.<sup>101</sup> In return for living quarters and eight dollars a month she was to "keep the rooms clean and open from 8 A.M. to 10 P.M. every day," and also to keep a record of periodicals, of books lent, and of daily attendance.<sup>102</sup> By July the reading room had 200 books, and for August the average daily attendance was reported to have been 55 5/7.<sup>103</sup>

Subscriptions were well paid that first year, with \$544.50 of the \$600 collected,<sup>104</sup> but after that it became "a question of great anxiety how to make ends meet."<sup>105</sup> An "elocutionary entertainment" brought in \$6.80; a benefit concert sponsored by C. G. Buckingham made \$1.50; a contribution box collected a few dollars a year; a lecture course was considered but was given up as "impracticable";

There was one other chance; the city might lend some support. On June 7, 1897, "the ladies of the Boulder Reading Room Association appeared before the Council and asked that the city appropriate \$100 to aid them."<sup>107</sup> The request was granted.<sup>108</sup> Within the next year a larger room was obtained,<sup>109</sup> some men were elected to the board,<sup>110</sup> and the constitution was revised to provide that members should be not merely stockholders but all who should sign the constitution and by-laws.<sup>111</sup> Although the city council then named two representatives on the board<sup>112</sup> and granted an allowance of fifty dollars a month,<sup>113</sup> the reading room still was a private institution, not administered by the city government.

The next development was a campaign to convert the reading room into a public library. With encouragement coming from a newly formed state library board,<sup>114</sup> local citizens petitioned the city council to establish a library, and they also asked that the city provide a building.<sup>115</sup> One citizen, who lamented in 1901 that Boulder had no public library, added:

"Private enterprise, however, has supplied the best substitute possible in the public reading room. Inaugurated by a few public spirited ladies and supported for years by the paltry half dollar subscriptions obtained through their humiliating but persistent solicitations, this institution has most economically and creditably fulfilled its mission and served, in a measure, to shield the city from any possible imputation of neglect." He then asked, "Shall this effort so worthily begun be abandoned?" and replied that he believed the reading room should be made the nucleus of a public library.<sup>116</sup>

In 1901 the city council voted to make the fifty dollar month-by-month allowance a regular appropriation<sup>117</sup> and then

bought and turned over to the reading room the 500 volume library of the Labor Union.<sup>118</sup> When a union member protested to the council that he would be charged a dollar to use the library, the secretary of the board of the reading room promptly promised that thenceforth all books of the "Free Reading Room" should "be circulated free of any and all charge."<sup>119</sup> By four successive steps, namely, the appointment of council members to the board, the annual city appropriation, the city's acquisition of books, and the elimination of fees, the reading room by 1901 had been converted into a public library.<sup>120</sup>

In 1903 L. C. Paddock, editor of the Camera, began waging a campaign for a library building. He related this to his simultaneous opposition to prohibition by arguing that with \$37,000 annual revenue from "liquid sources" (saloon licenses), the municipality was financially able to erect a library building. Moreover, "Within such a building the Y.M.C.A. or some kindred organization might equip a gymnasium or club rooms for the young people of the town. Some place for our boys to put in a comfortable evening with uplifting intellectual, decent environment."<sup>121</sup> He observed that Fort Collins was receiving a gift of \$10,000 from Andrew Carnegie for a library building by agreeing to spend \$1,000 a year for

The provision of public parks was animated by a desire to contribute positively to the general welfare by securing facilities for individual improvement, as in the case of the library, and also by contributing to the general beautification of the city. Prior to 1898 the town acquired and then relinquished one small city park--the public square. The shareholders in the original townsite had set aside a town square, for which the title later passed to the Town of Boulder.<sup>135</sup> Then the board of trustees had trees set out and

and had a chain strung around the lot on wooden posts.<sup>136</sup>

Maintenance of the public square was neglected,<sup>137</sup> and in 1881 the trustees proposed to subdivide it and sell the lots in order "to pay off a good deal of the town's indebtedness." To this the editor of the Herald added that it would also increase the revenue from taxation and provide construction work for idle men.<sup>138</sup> Immediately the future of the square became a live issue. Lewis Cheney offered \$10,000 for a 100-foot lot; A. J. Macky advised the town board to sell; John A. Ellet said sell for \$30,000 and buy a larger park elsewhere for \$15,000; the editor of the Herald protested that an outlying park would not be so desirable; and Dr. Charles Ambrook exclaimed, "Sell? Close up the streets, why don't you, so as to get rooms overhead to rent!"<sup>139</sup>

Since the county commissioners were at that time planning a new court house, sentiment turned in favor of preserving the town square for the site.<sup>140</sup> Immediately several business men formed a park association, raised

\$1,000, and obtained permission of the town board "to take charge and control of the square for the purpose of beautifying the same."<sup>141</sup> Next a question arose as to the validity of the town's title to the square,<sup>142</sup> and after that was settled beyond a doubt by obtaining a new deed,<sup>143</sup> the town granted the county perpetual use of the square as the site for the court house erected in 1883.<sup>144</sup>

The city was without a public park until 1898.<sup>145</sup> Although there was some discussion of the need for a park earlier,<sup>146</sup> it was the founding of the Chautauqua which brought results.<sup>147</sup> Since the city agreed to provide the site and buildings, when the Texas-Colorado Chautauqua Association selected the Bachelder tract south of town and

asked for an option on the adjoining Russell-Austin tract, the city council submitted two propositions to the electorate.<sup>148</sup> One asked approval for the municipal acquisition of parks, while the other asked authorization to issue bonds for their purchase and improvement, and both carried almost unanimously in April, 1898.<sup>149</sup>

This approval also gave sanction to the proposal that the city purchase a mountain tract from the government.<sup>150</sup> Consequently the city almost simultaneously purchased the Bachelder tract for the Chautauqua and a government tract of eighty acres on the east slope of Flagstaff Mountain for a mountain park.<sup>151</sup>

That spring an auditorium and dining hall on the Chautauqua grounds were rushed to completion in time for the opening of the season's program on July 4,<sup>152</sup> and that fall the city council formally adopted names for the newly acquired parks. The Chautauqua grounds were named Texado

Park, combining the words Texas and Colorado, and in the mountain park, to be known as Flagstaff Mountain, the park grounds were given the name American Park. In addition names were adopted for two other tracts not previously considered as parks. One, the city dump east of town, was to be known as Valverdan Park, and the other, the tract surrounding the reservoir in Sunshine Canon, was named Washington Park.<sup>153</sup>

After the initial step in the acquisition of two types of parks, city and mountain, others of both types were acquired and developed in succeeding years. This was engineered by two groups. One was the Boulder Improvement Society, a private organization founded in 1890 and revived in 1903 "to make Boulder more beautiful, more healthful, and in every way more desirable as an educational centre, a tourist point, a sanitarium and a city of homes."<sup>154</sup> This society engaged W. W. Parce, eminent landscape architect, to prepare plans for the beautification of Texado Park, and his

Another of the same name was founded in 1903 "to cooperate with all other organizations and individuals engaged in the work of bettering and beautifying the town." The officers were Junius Henderson, president; Eben G. Fine, vice-president; Wm. J. Baird, secretary, and Fred White, treasurer. Ibid., Feb. 20, 1903; Improvement Society papers, in University of Colorado Historical Collections.

In 1908 the Improvement Society brought to Boulder an outstanding city planning consultant, Frederick Law Olmsted, who by 1910 had prepared extensive recommendations for the improvement of the city streets, for development of additional city parks, for the control and beautification of the creek and for better sewage disposal.<sup>157</sup> This and the plans drawn by W. W. Parce provided the foundation for the labors of a second local group,<sup>158</sup> an official park board,

After the park board was established it labored diligently for improvements at the Chautauqua grounds and for some also on the adjoining Russell-Austin tract,<sup>163</sup> for which the option had been taken up in 1903.<sup>164</sup> Then it undertook to make something better of the dump and trash heap known as the "railroad park."<sup>165</sup> Permission for this had been granted by the Colorado and Southern Railway in 1906. The tract was leased and improved in 1921, followed by purchase in 1933; some adjoining lots were acquired after 1915; and the band shell was built by the Lions Club in 1938.<sup>166</sup>

memorial park.<sup>175</sup> This was approved in November, 1919, by a very small margin.<sup>176</sup> However, while the park commission was proceeding with plans to acquire old Gamble Field, south of the creek and east of Twelfth Street, and to make of it a well equipped recreation center,<sup>177</sup> for which only half of the approved sum was to be used at that time, public sentiment began to turn against spending even that amount. mountain parks. After the first eighty acre tract had been purchased in 1898<sup>180</sup> the city council responded to the suggestion of C. M. Campbell, then city attorney, that an appeal be sent to Congress for a gift of government land for park purposes.<sup>181</sup> Within a year Congress passed a bill granting Boulder 1800 acres of land extending from the top of Flagstaff mountain west to the mouth of Four Mile Creek and from Sunshine Canon south to Green Mountain.<sup>182</sup> Immediately some adjoining school land was acquired from the state,<sup>183</sup> and this was followed by more negotiations with the government for the gift and purchase of additional tracts.<sup>184</sup> Some other privately owned tracts were bought in order to prevent the Red Rocks and the Flatirons from being marred by stone quarries or other commercial use.<sup>185</sup> In addition in

Buckingham contributed a mining claim at Boulder Falls in 1914 and another in Left Hand Canon fifteen years later.<sup>187</sup> Finally, in 1919, in order to protect the Silver Lake watershed, the city bought nearly 4,000 acres of government land, including Arapahoe Glacier, and thus Boulder gained the distinction of being the "city which owns a glacier."<sup>188</sup>

By virtue of all these gifts, purchases, and labors Boulder climbed rapidly to a preeminent position as a city of parks. In 1920 the city owned nearly 3,000 acres of parks besides another 2,000 acres of watershed land, and the city parks and improvements along with the mountain lands were valued at about \$127,000.<sup>197</sup> By 1928 Eben G. Fine could claim for Boulder the "distinction of having the largest acreage of municipally owned mountain park lands of any city of its size in the world--something over 6,000 acres."<sup>198</sup> In 1932 there were sixty-two acres of parks within the city and 6,362 outside the city limits, making one acre for each two persons in Boulder.<sup>199</sup> At that time, according to Mrs. Louise C. Cheney, only two cities, Denver and Phoenix, owned

196. Ibid., April 15, 1937.

a larger acreage.<sup>200</sup> By 1946 Boulder owned 143 acres of city parks, 5,380 acres of mountain parks, and 6,000 acres of watershed land, besides an airport of about 160 acres and a golf course of 133 acres.<sup>201</sup>

The work of the park commission for the beautification of Boulder led naturally to the adoption of a city zoning ordinance in 1928. For years there had been some restrictions upon land use within the city, and also upon the posting of bills, both of which were feeble efforts in the direction of zoning.<sup>202</sup> When "violent objections" arose in 1926 against the "promiscuous erection of places of business in what has been the residence district on the Hill," the city attorney ruled that restrictions could not be adopted for one part of the city alone.<sup>203</sup> Consequently the park

commission employed S. R. De Boer, eminent planning consultant, to prepare a complete zoning resolution.<sup>204</sup> In 1927 he drafted a proposal which divided the city into seven districts, each with its own limitations upon building, land use, and lot area. There were three types of residential districts, two commercial zones, one industrial area, and a business section, and a committee of five was to be appointed by the mayor to hear appeals.<sup>205</sup> With minor changes, his recommendations were adopted in January, 1928.<sup>206</sup>

The Planning and Parks Commission, sitting in special sessions and with a different chairman, then became the appeal board, and each year it passed upon several requests for permission to deviate from the regulations.<sup>207</sup> In 1933, for example, it heard ten appeals, of which two were withdrawn, one rejected, and seven granted subject to the approval of adjoining property owners.<sup>208</sup> In 1943 this commission also made recommendations to the county commissioners for the zoning of the city's fringe in the

In 1917 the Charter provided that all municipal activities corresponding to those mentioned above, along with any charitable and correctional activities of the city, should be brought together in a Department of Public Welfare, supported by a tax levy of not less than one-half mill.<sup>222</sup> This support was augmented by the cooperation of the park commission, which with aid from benefit baseball games<sup>223</sup> and other sources equipped playgrounds in Barker Park in 1918-1922,<sup>224</sup> in North Boulder Park in 1936,<sup>225</sup> and in Beach Park in 1938,<sup>226</sup> as well as tennis courts and a lighted softball field on park lands along the creek in 1930-1932.<sup>227</sup>

### CHAPTER SIX TROUBLES ALWAYS

A municipal government, being one of man's creations to serve his needs, deals primarily with people, both as individuals and as the public-at-large. This involves the element of "human nature," and as one citizen once remarked, "there is considerable of it in Boulder."<sup>1</sup> In addition a municipal organization has valuable property and equipment to manage, and sooner or later ways have to be found to pay for both the capital outlay and the operating expense. Moreover, in a changeable climate the unpredictable elements sometimes play havoc with man's designs, and in a troubled world some international crises disturb the serenity of even the most remote communities. Indeed, there are troubles always.

Most apparent among the many difficulties of administration are those which are aired in the courts. The suits involving the town and city of Boulder, which have been mentioned already, have included these: The suit for payment of the defaulted Denver and Boulder Valley Rail Road bonds in 1875 to 1878;<sup>2</sup> the restraining order which temporarily blocked consideration of the building of a hydroelectric

1. O. H. Wangelin, in Herald, Feb. 15, 1888.
2. Chapter III. In 1880 when four old BVRR bonds turned up, settlement was made on the same basis as effected by the earlier judgment. Town Minutes, April 13, 1880. In 1909 when another one of those bonds appeared, the city council was at loss what to do about it. Camera April 23, 1909.

plant;<sup>3</sup> the unsuccessful effort to declare the election of prohibitionists illegal in 1907;<sup>4</sup> the legal contest which held up the paving of Mapleton Avenue in 1923;<sup>5</sup> and the suit to require that the city submit the Memorial Park question to a referendum in 1921.<sup>6</sup> In addition an account of the violent attacks on the city manager plan from 1922 to 1925 has been included previously.<sup>7</sup>

Some other legal contests deserve mention. In 1896 when the city engineer reported that the Fleetwood Construction Company of Denver was not complying with the specifications for the construction of sewers, the city council did not accept the work and cancelled the contract. Then the construction company brought suit and won a small judgment against the city. The city had to pay that while also mending the faulty sewer joints and supervising continuation of the construction.<sup>8</sup> A few years later the O'Brien Construction Company quit work upon the Silver Lake dam by order of the state engineer. Then with the contention that the work complied with the city specifications, which were not in accord with state

3. Chapter III.

4. Chapter V.

5. Chapter II.

6. Chapter V.

7. Chapter II.

8. Council Minutes, Jan. 16, 23, 29, April 20, Nov. 2, Dec. 22, 1896; June 7, Oct. 5, 1897.

requirements, the construction company initiated suit against the city. This claim was settled in 1914 by the payment of \$4,000 to the company for work done and for materials acquired.<sup>9</sup>

In 1899 neighbors of the old dumping ground east of the city, after complaining that it had become a nuisance, obtained a court decree permanently restraining the city from any further use of that tract for that purpose.<sup>10</sup> A few years later, in 1905, another decree temporarily checked the city's acquisition of Silver Lake. The protestants objected to the first agreement with James P. Maxwell on the ground that no municipality could assume the legal obligations of any person or corporation, in this case the rights and obligations of the Silver Lake Ditch Company. A plan to obviate this limitation by separating the ditch rights and lake storage rights was accepted by the court.<sup>11</sup>

A series of legal difficulties arose from a "taxi war" in 1934. The city manager revoked the license of a scenic car operator whose charges against competitors had not been sustained in court. Then this operator continued in business and in the resulting suit the operator's contention that the city had practiced discrimination and had altered the records

9. Camera, June 7, 1912; Council Minutes, Sept. 1, 15, 1914.

10. Ibid., April 20, 1896; Oct. 4, 1899; April 16, 1900; News, Sept. 28, 1899.

11. Camera, Dec. 28, 1905; Jan. 3, 16, 25, 30, 1906; Jan. 25, 1906.

also were not upheld.<sup>12</sup> Shortly afterward the partner of that operator was called down by a police officer and in the ensuing brawl was subdued by the patrolman and the police chief and then jailed and fined for disturbing the peace. Since he had suffered a broken finger in the melee, he then brought suit for \$15,000 damages against the two police officers for using "greater force than was necessary in the course of their duty." The court exonerated the patrolman but awarded a judgment of \$400 against the chief, then no longer in office, and since this was not a suit against the city, the former chief, aided by his friends and by his bonding company, had to pay the judgment and costs.<sup>13</sup>

In the 1930's some test cases resulted from the efforts of Boulder druggists and restaurant owners to require the city to follow state law with regard to liquor licenses, rather than the local ordinances adopted under the Charter. However, the state supreme court held that the legislation of municipalities under charter rule should prevail.<sup>14</sup>

Every once in a while there has been a suit against the city for damages as a result of injuries received from a fall on the sidewalk or from some accident involving city

12. Ibid., Dec. 3, 29, 1934; Jan. 28, April 26, 1935; May 25, 1936; May 4, 1938.

property.<sup>15</sup> In one such instance in 1891, after a lady had suffered injuries by falling over a loose board on a sidewalk, the Camera commented: "Rome will howl if those nails are not driven in and the city will find that it has been leaking at the bung while trying to save at the spigot." In 1937 a Denver woman sued the city for \$20,700 damages after her car had hit a ridge of gravel,<sup>16</sup> but this case was dismissed. However in another case, after the state supreme court had awarded \$4,500 damages to a lady who had tumbled over a park bench on the sidewalk, the city officials were greatly concerned lest this establish a precedent for a "possible flood of litigation against municipalities in the future."<sup>17</sup>

Since 1890 there has been a growing tendency for the damage cases to be settled out of court. Claims filed with the city attorney or presented to the city council are either rejected or adjusted.<sup>18</sup> Several of the claims have been

filed by one local attorney, who seems to have taken up the specialty of seeking damages for the victims of accidents.<sup>19</sup>

The handling of the claims and suits, as well as other legal matters of the municipality, is the function of the city attorney. The very first ordinances of the town provided that the town board at its first meeting each year should appoint an attorney on a part-time basis to "prosecute all suits," to execute judgments, to draft ordinances, bonds, and contracts, and to act as legal adviser.<sup>20</sup> There have been revisions since then, but no essential change, and in 1919, Frank L. Moorhead, who has been city attorney since 1916, reported his year's work for the city as follows: Prepared forty-seven ordinances, twenty-six resolutions, and nine contracts; examined nine bonds; attended six court hearings, appeared before the Public Utilities Commission ten times; examined the records in eleven matters, attended thirty meetings of the council and twenty-three meetings of department heads; held 167 consultations; and handled the records for Improvement District No. 11, then being developed.<sup>21</sup> This is representative of the work of one who

19. Ibid., Dec. 21, 1926; Dec. 18, 1928; August 4, 1942; Oct. 3, 1944; et passim.

Greene, 1878-1885, 1890-1, 1892-6; David Bartlett, 1886-7; C. M. Campbell, 1887-8, 1896-1903; R. H. Whiteley, 1888-90, 1891-2; E. C. Mason, 1903-5; H. O. Andrew, 1905-7; A. A. Reed, 1907-11; J. T. Atwood, 1911-13; H. E. Rowland, 1913-15; and M. C. Goss, 1916.

guards the municipality against legal pitfalls.

Another type of difficulty arose from crises, local and national. In this category municipal response to the local epidemics, to the critical water shortage, and to the serious unemployment of the 1930's has come in for previous discussion.<sup>22</sup> But there have been other catastrophes and crises.

The flood of May 1894, although quite serious,<sup>23</sup> did not loom large in the proceedings of the city council. The first hint that something had gone wrong came on June 12 in the form of a resolution to repair and "put in place" the bridge on Arapahoe Street by Highland School.<sup>24</sup> Next, on July 2, the city council called for bids "for removing the 9th, 12th, 17th and Arapahoe bridges, and all parts belonging thereto, from the creek to the banks where needed."<sup>25</sup> In addition, in a long list of bills to be paid there was one for \$145.43 "for flood expenses," and unusually large amounts were spent for labor, hauling, lumber, and "sax."<sup>26</sup> Evidently the city had bought all the sacks in town, for sand bags.

A little later there were some entries about bids for new bridges, and finally on October 1 an ordinance was adopted "declaring the necessity of certain improvements by

22. Chapter IV, III, and V, respectively.

23. Camera, May 31, 1894.

24. Minutes, June 12, 1894.

25. Ibid., July 2, 1894.

26. Ibid.

reason of a casualty in the City of Boulder and making an appropriation therefore."<sup>27</sup> The amount of the appropriation was \$10,000, for seven new bridges and for "general repair of streets." Almost a year later steps were taken to build a barrier along the banks of the creek because of the "danger of another overflow."<sup>28</sup> In the meantime one J. Faus had "appeared before the council and asked pay for his lot washed away by the flood last May," and his claim had been referred to the judiciary committee.<sup>29</sup>

Three later floods, in 1914, in 1919, and in 1921, did little damage in the city. However, because they broke the water transmission lines in the canon, the residents of parts of the city had to be served temporarily by the city sprinkler, while many obtained water at the Sanitarium spring and at the Chautauqua well.<sup>30</sup>

The first World War also created emergency conditions. Several municipal employes and candidates for office were taken away, either for service in the army or by the attraction of more lucrative employment.<sup>31</sup> To counteract the latter tendency and to meet the increased cost of living the

27. Ordinances, No. 259, Oct. 1, 1894.

28. Council Minutes, March 4, April 5, 1895; Feb. 5, 1896; March 6, 1899.

29. Ibid., Nov. 8, 1894.

30. Camera, June 2, 3, 1914; August 1, 2, 1919; June 7, 13, 1921.

31. Ibid., August 8, 1914; May 17, 1916; April 30, 1917; Feb. 21, Sept. 9, Oct. 28, 1918; June 19, 1919.

city council was forced to raise salaries and wages.<sup>32</sup> In addition, in order to encourage the raising of foodstuffs, the city at first offered free water for backyard gardens and later fixed the rate at two cents per foot of garden space.<sup>33</sup> The war delayed construction of water lines and of new paving,<sup>34</sup> and it indirectly required the employing of extra policemen to halt the wave of burglaries.<sup>35</sup> The police department was also flooded with anonymous complaints about the alleged pro-German sentiments of local citizens.<sup>36</sup> Moreover, the influenza epidemics at times practically closed all city offices.<sup>37</sup> Finally, after the war had ended, the city for awhile operated an emergency employment office at the Central Fire Station, to help place returning soldiers in jobs.<sup>38</sup>

The second World War required some similar steps and others which were more drastic. Immediately after the entrance of the United States into the war, a local civilian defense council was set up with top city officials filling

the key positions.<sup>39</sup> The council enlisted and trained volunteer air raid wardens, fire watchers, auxiliary policemen, and public works crews, and held trial blackouts.<sup>40</sup> The council also directed a campaign for the salvaging of critical materials,<sup>41</sup> and for a few months the city manager had the added responsibility of making a survey of the needs for critical materials for the War Production Board.<sup>42</sup> As a special precaution the city employed armed guards to patrol the city water reservoirs.<sup>43</sup>

Again the war caused a considerable exodus of employes,<sup>44</sup> which again led to wage and salary increases to try to hold those tempted to accept war jobs.<sup>45</sup> The city employes adopted a pay-roll deduction plan for the purchase of war bonds,<sup>46</sup> and city funds which had accumulated due to the halt on construction work were also invested in war bonds.<sup>47</sup> In addition, as in the other war, a special

inducement of half-rate for water was offered to encourage Victory gardening.<sup>48</sup>

With the opening of the Navy Japanese Language School at the University, the city council approved admitting Japanese instructors, students, and families to residence in Boulder,<sup>49</sup> and later, because of the large enrollment in officers' training at the University, and in view of the limitations imposed by gasoline rationing, the city liberalized its policy with respect to Sunday amusements.<sup>50</sup> Likewise, student training in aviation called for expansion and improvements at the municipal airport.<sup>51</sup>

As the war was near its end and Vice-President Harry S. Truman assumed the mantle laid down by President Franklin D. Roosevelt, the city council adopted a resolution endorsing Truman's expressed determination to prosecute the war to complete victory and "to build a strong and lasting United Nations organization at the San Francisco Conference for mastering the problems of peace through the triumphs of justice."<sup>52</sup> Prior to that the council had appropriated \$100 towards tokens and gifts for the crew of a Victory ship named after Boulder,<sup>53</sup> and later the council was informed that the

48. Ibid., March 2, 1943; Camera, March 17, 1943.

49. Council Minutes, July 21, 1942; July 20, Sept. 21, 1943.

50. Camera, Nov. 4, 1942.

51. Ibid., June 16; Council Minutes, Jan. 4, 1944.

52. Ibid., April 17, 1945.

53. Ibid., June 20, 1944.

"City of Boulder," a "superfortress" based in the Marianas had a record which was "a credit to your community."<sup>54</sup>

Upon the conclusion of the war, when Boulder suddenly faced a critical shortage of housing facilities, the city tried to make an appreciable contribution to the solution of that problem. Application was made to the government for allocation to the City of Boulder of 200 housing units, and a few were allotted, while the city officials also converted the auto camp to a park for house trailers and sought a solution to the problem of "winterizing" the privately owned cottages at the Chautauqua in order to prepare them for year 'round occupancy.<sup>55</sup>

Certain other difficulties arose to plague the city's administrators from time to time whenever events took some unexpected turn. This happened once, for example, in 1883, when a row over politics and saloons led to the sudden resignation of the mayor,<sup>56</sup> and again when the first city manager resigned unexpectedly after a brief term,<sup>57</sup> and again when a part-time welfare director lost his principal job and

54. Named by Captain Wm. P. Sullivan after his home town. Letter from Colonel of the Air Corps, George W. Mundy, ibid., June 5, 1945.

57. E. O. Heinrich. Camera, April 2, 1919.

committed suicide.<sup>58</sup>

Other surprising developments were less serious, as, for example, when the city council made an appropriation to one University publication only to have another unheard-of University publication apply for one,<sup>59</sup> and when the council gave financial aid to one reading room and then discovered that there was another reading room in town wanting like consideration.<sup>60</sup> Moreover, once a local laundry sent the city a bill for \$7.50 "for expense of hauling water while city water was muddy,"<sup>61</sup> and at one council meeting it was reported that "Mr. N. H. McCall's steer was impounded and sold to Mr. Fred Burger. The steer got away from Mr. Burger's possession and went back home, to Mr. McCall's, who now has the steer in his possession and will not give it up. Mr. Burger was present and asked the Council to give him his money back, but the Council thought it proper to have nothing to do with it."<sup>62</sup>

On another occasion an engineering professor at the University was reported to have written a "letter which floored the council.... He told the aldermen he wouldn't lay

He quoted Blackstone and all the reports and laid down constitutional law."<sup>63</sup> Some little trouble was caused once when a carload of worthless cement was purchased by the city and some work had to be done all over,<sup>64</sup> when the dump was found to be overrun with disease-ridden rats and a man had to be employed to exterminate them,<sup>65</sup> and when some pranksters sawed off the flagpole on Flagstaff Mountain and another had to be obtained and erected.<sup>66</sup> Then too, there is always someone who wants an ordinance adopted immediately about something or other, as in the case of one councilman who came in with such a request after he had seen some gasoline dripping from an automobile onto the city's brand new bitulithic pavement.<sup>67</sup>

It was probably amusing if not a bit embarrassing to the council when a prominent lady came before that august body to advocate an appropriation for child welfare, only to get into a lively quarrel over that with a woman member of the council,<sup>68</sup> and likewise when the auditor undertook to criticize the bookkeeping of the director of finance and

records and provoked a caustic retort.<sup>69</sup> It was not funny, however, when the city's twelve-ton auto sprinkler fell through the Ninth Street bridge;<sup>70</sup> or when a wood fire started by the Fire Department got out of control and burned over fifteen acres before it was checked,<sup>71</sup> or when a severe cold spell froze 350 city water lines, keeping four electric thawing machines going constantly,<sup>72</sup> or when an inspection of Green Mountain lodge revealed that someone had broken thirty-one windows,<sup>73</sup> or when the city manager received notice that the State Division of Public Health would "be obliged to take action" if the city did not improve the "degree of treatment" of sewage at the modern disposal plant erected in accordance with state requirements.<sup>74</sup>

There have been a few occasions when city officials had to resign because of inefficiency or misconduct,<sup>75</sup> and the city once had a mayor who provoked the wrath of several department heads, one of whom resigned. This mayor, who was reported to have required the city engineer to report to him weekly and not to leave town during business hours without consent, tried unsuccessfully to dismiss the engineer, and

A most critical situation arose in the official family later, right in the midst of the turmoil over continuation of the city manager form of government and of the lawsuits over the paving of the city streets.<sup>78</sup> First, as a result of a feud between the sheriff's office and the chief of police the sheriff and five city policemen asked the city manager to oust the chief, whereupon the city manager dismissed the five patrolmen for insubordination.<sup>79</sup> Presently one of those patrolmen was murdered mysteriously,<sup>80</sup> and then the chief and an alleged accomplice were held, as the suspected murderers, upon orders of the district attorney.<sup>81</sup> The city manager, however, maintained that the chief was still chief until duly convicted.<sup>82</sup> After some sensational trials in which it became apparent that these charges were just another maneuver

Not the least of the worries of the city's administrators has been the problem of paying the bills. The first town ordinances provided that the town clerk should keep a record of all warrants issued, and if any were presented without funds to pay them, they should be taken care of according to the "priority of registration."<sup>85</sup> The clerk was also required to make out at the end of each fiscal year, March 31, "a full and explicit statement of receipts and expenditures," and at that time the anticipated receipts were taxes, fines, license fees, and processing fees.<sup>86</sup> The report of the first clerk, on April 6, 1872, showed \$65.00 received from liquor licenses, \$9.00 from dog licenses and \$15.00 from fines, while expenditures, mostly for printing, were \$106.10. Warrants outstanding then totalled \$805.08 for other expenditures, and no taxes had been received yet. Therefore the current deficit was \$822.15.<sup>87</sup> The assessed valuation was \$252,326, against which taxes were being levied at the rate of one mill for city officers, one for streets,

83. Ibid., Feb. 2-6, 1924; Oct. 29, 1925.

84. Camera, Feb. 6, 1924.

85. Town Minutes, Nov. 11, 1871.

86. Ibid.

87. Ibid., April 6, 1872.

walks, and bridges, and four for interest on the public debt (railroad bonds).<sup>88</sup>

The next year, in March, A. J. Macky, clerk, reported that he had received \$482.00 for licenses, \$19.75 in fines, \$37.95 for sidewalks assessments, and \$299.05 in taxes collected. With annual expenditures of \$1,746.84, he had to count taxes not yet collected to the amount of \$1,214.91 in order to show a balance of \$306.82.<sup>89</sup> Soon the town incurred more indebtedness, by issuing bonds for \$18,000 to start building a water system, and the rate of interest paid was ten per cent.<sup>90</sup> By March 31, 1877, A. J. Macky reported that the total indebtedness, including water bonds, warrants still out, and so on, was \$34,628.91, but he showed the total assets, including cash, equipment, and property, to be \$36,903.64.<sup>91</sup> The debt, then, was about five per cent. of the total assessed valuation,<sup>92</sup> and once when the town borrowed \$2,000 from John A. Ellet in order to pay current bills the clerk gave him in return town water bonds figured at eighty cents on the dollar with "accumulated interest thrown in."<sup>93</sup>

Apparently the town finances were none too sound, and this was not helped any by the expensive suit which required the paying of defaulted railroad bonds beginning in 1880.<sup>94</sup> Neither was this helped any by the tendency of many of the leading taxpayers to allow their taxes to go delinquent, and in 1877 the town board undertook to improve collections by ordering property sold if the taxes were not paid.<sup>95</sup>

In 1878 a special committee of the council made a study of the financial situation. It ordered inventories of the street and water departments, inquired about the revenue from all sources, and reported that of the taxes levied in the preceding October, \$3,596.15 had been collected by that February and \$4,073.08 remained unpaid.<sup>96</sup> That same year, in order to pay the bills, the town borrowed \$4,000 from the town clerk, A. J. Macky, and as security gave him \$6,000 in town bonds besides paying him ten per cent. interest on the borrowed money.<sup>97</sup> Moreover, when the town sent warrants

In 1880 they adopted a resolution ordering the county treasurer to stop taking in "any obligations of the Town in payment of taxes due the Town," and added that "cash will be expected in payment of all such taxes."<sup>99</sup> It was at that time, too, that the trustees considered selling the town square in order to lift some of the public debt.<sup>100</sup>

From 1878 to 1885, the period in which the town was reincorporated under state law, first as a town and then as a city, a serious effort was made to improve the municipal finances. First came the practice of adopting annual appropriation ordinances. The initial one, for 1880, allowed \$400 for interest on water bonds, \$1,000 for repair of the water works, \$1,000 and all poll tax receipts for street construction and repairs, \$200 for fire department expenses, \$250 for office supplies, \$100 for printing, \$2,700 for

98. Ibid., May 5, 1879. When a supply house pressed for payment of a bill for \$560 for water department materials, the trustees paid it with \$500 diverted from an appropriation for ditch shares and \$60 taken from the license fund. Ibid., Feb. 4, 1880.

salaries, \$800 for a note and interest, \$3,000 for warrants of 1877 and earlier, \$5,924.42 for payment on the railroad bond judgment, and \$1,000 for "all else."<sup>101</sup> A few years later the city council requested the city clerk to keep a record of appropriations, to debit each expenditure from the proper fund, and to make quarterly reports as to the balances in the several funds.<sup>102</sup> In this way budgetting was introduced, but one peculiar feature then and for years afterward was that an outgoing administration in its last month in office drew up an appropriation ordinance for the succeeding administration to follow.

Another innovation came in 1884 when the council's finance committee was authorized "to employ an expert to examine the Books of the City Treasurer...", and in March, 1885, E. L. Austin reported that the accorunts were "all correct."<sup>103</sup> This introduced the plan of having regular audits. In addition the town attorney was asked to study the procedure followed in drawing warrants and to devise some plan" for a more just and perfect manner of payment...."<sup>104</sup>

The treasurer's pay was reduced to one dollar a month, because the "opinion among the aldermen seemed to be that a treasurer had a right to use the funds as his own just so he gave a good bond and paid the warrants as presented." To this the Herald added that, although it was dangerous, "banks speculate with the funds, why not a private individual so long as his bond is good?"<sup>111</sup>

Since 1907 local improvment bonds, mostly for paving, had been issued to the sum of \$1,750,000, but like the water bonds, they were not a general public obligation.<sup>125</sup> However, they were administered by the city department of finance and records, and, as pointed out previously, retirement of them was provided for by a special assessment against the property owners in each district.<sup>126</sup> All went well until the depression of the 1930's, when, with much delinquency in the paying of taxes, funds did not come in adequately for retiring the bonds and for paying the interest when due.<sup>127</sup> Although a plan was devised for cashing tax sale certificates, this still did not provide a full remedy and at the end of 1939 the total of these bonds in default was \$62,200.<sup>128</sup> By 1945 tax sale certificates totalling \$70,409.70 were held in trust by the city for the owners of outstanding bonds, and the amount outstanding then had been reduced to \$120,900.<sup>129</sup>

Obviously the troubles about keeping the bills paid have been reduced considerably in recent yers. Nevertheless, with higher salaries and expenses and with demands for further expansion of services, there was not enough money to go around. In 1943 the city manager, H. C. McClintock, stated that "if Boulder is to maintain the type of service her citizens desire, and be permitted to progress with the times, it is imperative that the city council be given more discretion in the determination of the annual tax levy."<sup>137</sup> Then he listed the many needs, as follows: Resurfacing of old paving, replacement of obsolete equipment, extension of modern street lighting, a pump truck for the University Hill fire station, more policemen, renovation of the Chautauqua buildings, funds for maintenance of the many parks donated to

134. Camera, Oct. 5, 1940.

135. Biography in the Appendix.

136. April 7, 1926.

137. Camera, August 4, 1943.

the city, additional recreation leaders, another employe in the health department, increased wages to meet the higher cost of living, and funds for an employes' pension plan.<sup>138</sup>

The Charter, however, fixed the maximum tax levy at ten mills for the general expenses of municipal government.<sup>139</sup> Therefore in 1943 an amendment raising the limit to thirteen mills was submitted to the elctorate and was approved by a small margin.<sup>140</sup> Consequently that year the city council was able to raise the rate of levy to eleven and two tenths mills, which on a valuation of \$10,290,780 made available about \$115,000 for 1944.<sup>141</sup>

Years ago, when an oil company promised to pay the city liberal royalties for a lease to drill on city-owned land, it was "fondly anticipated by the city fathers that at no distant day all taxes may be omitted and the city government run out of the proceeds of its oil lands."<sup>142</sup> In 1926, when another oil prospector tried again, it was hoped that under its agreement with the city it might return "enough royalty to pay all city expenses."<sup>143</sup> However, in neither case did the hope materialize. Later, in 1934, when Dr. William J.

139. The limit of general indebtedness also was fixed at 3% of the assessed valuation. Charter of the City of Boulder, 1917, pages 25-26.
